Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
8 min
The Emergence of Sects According to al-Maqrizi
Al-Maqrizi chronologically lists deviant sects from Qadariyya to Mujassima

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the chronological emergence of major deviant sects in early Islam as recorded by al-Maqrizi, and how each sect represented an extreme reaction against the orthodox position.

Core Idea
Deviant sects emerged gradually as reactions to each other and to orthodoxy.

Al-Maqrizi provides a concise historical survey of how sectarian divisions arose after the early Islamic period. This lesson guides you through his chronological account, from the first free-will advocates to the Corporealists, showing how each deviation sparked a counter-deviation. By following his narrative, you can see how the early community's middle path was challenged from multiple directions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the sequence of sects as they appear. Notice the names of founders and key doctrines mentioned.
The passage uses ellipses (...) to indicate abridgment; focus on the main structure.
2
Identify each sect and its founder
As you read, list the six sects in order: Qadariyya (Ma'bad), Khawarij, Shi'a extremists, Jahmiyya (Jahm), Mu'tazila (al-Hasan al-Basri), and Mujassima (Ibn Karram). Note what each group believed and how they deviated from orthodoxy.
3
Notice the chronological and causal links
Observe how al-Maqrizi presents each sect emerging in a specific era, often as a reaction to the previous one. For example, the Mu'tazila opposed the Jahmiyya's negation, and the Mujassima overcorrected against the Mu'tazila.
This shows a pattern of theological pendulum swings.
4
Reflect on the orthodox position
Consider what the orthodox position must be: affirming attributes without likeness (against Jahmiyya and Mujassima), affirming divine justice without denying free will (against Qadariyya and Jabriyya), and maintaining community unity (against Khawarij and extremists).
Implicitly, al-Maqrizi defends the Sunni middle path.
5
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding with the multiple-choice question. Return to the passage if needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان أول من قال بالقدر معبد بن خالد الجهني.... ولما بلغ عبد الله الخطاب مقالة معبد في القدر تبرأ من القدرية...، وحدث أيضًا في زمن ابن عمر من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهب الخوارج، وصرَّحوا بالتكفير بالذنب والخروج على الإمام وقتاله، فناظرهم عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما فلم يرجعوا إلى الحق وقاتلهم أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه وقتل منهم جماعة كما هو معروف.... وحدث في زمن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهبُ التشيع لعلي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه والغلو فيه، فلما بلغه ذلك أنكره وحرَّق بالنار جماعة منهم ممن غلا فيه...، ثم حدث بعد عصر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهب جهم بن صفوان ببلاد المشرق، فعظمت الفتنة به، فإنه نفى أن يكون الله تعالى صفة، وأورد على أهل الإسلام شكوكًا أثرت في الملة الإسلامية آثارًا قبيحة تولد عنها بلاء كبير، وكان قبل المئة من سني الهجرة، فكثر أتباعه على أقواله التي تؤول إلى التعطيل .... وفي أثناء ذلك حدث مذهب الاعتزال منذ زمن الحسن بن الحسين البصري رحمه الله بعد المئتين من سني الهجرة، وألفوا في مسائل العدل والتوحيد وإثبات أفعال العباد وأن الله تعالى لا يخلق الشر، وجهروا بأن الله لا يُرى في الآخرة، وأنكروا عذاب القبر على البدن، وأعلنوا بأن القرءان مخلوق محدث، إلى غير ذلك من مسائلهم، فتبعهم خلائق في بدعهم.... ثم حدث مذهب التجسيم المضاد لمذهب الاعتزال، فظهر محمد بن كرام بن عراق السجستاني زعيم الطائفة الكرامية... وأثبت الصفات حتى انتهى فيها إلى التجسيم والتشبيه...» إلى آخر كلامه رحمه الله.
And the first who said about predestination was Ma'bad ibn Khalid al-Juhani.... When 'Abd Allah al-Khattab was informed of Ma'bad's statement about predestination, he disassociated himself from the Qadariyya... Also in the time of Ibn 'Umar among the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the doctrine of the Khawarij occurred; they openly declared takfir for sin and rebellion against the imam and fighting him. So 'Abd Allah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, debated them, but they did not return to the truth. The Commander of the Faithful 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, fought them and killed a group of them, as is well-known.... And in the time of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the doctrine of partisanship for 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, and extremism in him occurred. When that reached him, he denounced it and burned with fire a group of them who had exceeded bounds... Then after the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the doctrine of Jahm ibn Safwan occurred in the land of the east, and the tribulation became great because of him. He denied that Allah has any attribute, and brought forward doubts against the people of Islam which affected the Islamic nation with ugly effects from which a great calamity was born. That was before one hundred years of the Hijra, and his followers multiplied upon his sayings, which lead to negation... During that, the doctrine of the Mu'tazila occurred from the time of al-Hasan ibn al-Husayn al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him, after two hundred years of the Hijra. They authored works on the issues of justice, monotheism, affirming the actions of servants, and that Allah does not create evil. They declared that Allah will not be seen in the afterlife, denied the punishment of the grave upon the body, declared that the Qur'an is created and originated, and other such issues. Multitudes followed them in their innovations.... Then the doctrine of Corporealism occurred, opposed to the doctrine of the Mu'tazila. Muhammad ibn Karram ibn 'Iraq al-Sijistani, the leader of the Karramiyya sect, appeared... He affirmed the attributes until he ended up in anthropomorphism and likeness..." up to the end of his words, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a chronological enumeration of deviant sects, quoted from al-Maqrizi. The author uses this summary to show how sectarian divisions emerged gradually after the early Islamic period. It begins with the Qadariyya: Ma'bad al-Juhani was the first to advocate free will (qadar). Then the Khawarij (Kharijites) appeared in the time of Ibn 'Umar, declaring excommunication for major sins and legitimizing rebellion against the imam. Ibn 'Abbas debated them, and 'Ali fought them. Next came the Shia extremists (ghulat) who exaggerated in their love for 'Ali; 'Ali himself denounced them and burned some. After the Companions' era, Jahm ibn Safwan emerged in the East, denying all divine attributes (ta'til), causing great tribulation. Then the Mu'tazila arose after 200 AH, pioneered by al-Hasan al-Basri (though the text says al-Hasan ibn al-Husayn al-Basri, likely a corruption). They focused on justice, monotheism, free will, denied vision of God in the afterlife, denied bodily punishment in the grave, and declared the Qur'an created. Finally, the Mujassima (Corporealists) appeared as a counter-reaction, led by Muhammad ibn Karram, who affirmed attributes to the point of anthropomorphism. The passage thus presents a historical chain of deviations, each reacting to previous ones, and underscores that the early community was united on a middle path of affirmation without likeness and transcendence without negation.

Key Terms
قدرية
الخوارج
التشيع
الغلو
جهم بن صفوان
الاعتزال
التجسيم
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all sects appeared simultaneously.
Correction: The passage shows a clear chronological sequence: Qadariyya first, then Khawarij, Shi'a extremists, Jahmiyya, Mu'tazila, finally Mujassima.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maqrizi's summary, which sect was the first to emerge among those listed?
A) Mu'tazila
B) Jahmiyya
C) Qadariyya
D) Khawarij

Key Takeaway
The emergence of deviant sects followed a chronological pattern of reaction and counter-reaction, always in opposition to the middle path of the early community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own walk with Allah, where do you see a tendency to overcorrect? For example, if you fear falling into excessive love for the Prophet, do you find yourself downplaying his status? How will you stay on the middle path taught by the early community?
?
The Khawarij left the fold due to what they saw as a minor sin in others. Are there any sins in fellow Muslims that make you feel morally superior or distance yourself from them? How can you guard against that today?
?
Al-Maqrizi shows sects reacting to each other, each going to an extreme. Think of a religious issue on which you hold a strong opinion: are you reacting against an opposing extreme so strongly that you risk falling into the opposite error? What concrete step will you take to seek the balanced path?

The Rise of the Qadariyya
Qadariyya emergence and immediate rejection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the origins of the Qadariyya sect and the early response from the Companions.

Core Idea
The first theological deviation in Islam was the denial of divine predestination.

The passage begins by describing the peaceful era of the Companions, which was disrupted by the first notable theological debate: the question of al-Qadr (predestination). Ma'bad al-Juhani is identified as the first to speak about it, and his views were quickly rejected by Umar ibn al-Khattab. This lesson covers that initial controversy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key figures and terms.
Pay attention to the passive construction.
2
Understand the context
Recognize that this sect emerged during the time of the Companions.
3
Identify the deviation
Ma'bad al-Juhani's claim about al-Qadr is the first theological innovation.
The Companion Umar immediately disassociated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمضى عصر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم على هذا إلى أن حدث في زمنهم القول بالقدر.... وكان أول من قال بالقدر معبد بن خالد الجهني.... ولما بلغ عبد الله الخطاب مقالة معبد في القدر تبرأ من القدرية...، وحدث أيضًا في زمن ابن عمر بن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهب الخوارج، وصرَّحوا بالتكفير بالذنب والخروج على الإمام وقتاله، فناظرهم عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما فلم يرجعوا إلى الحق (۱) وقاتلهم أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه وقتل منهم جماعة كما هو معروف.... وحدث في زمن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهبُ التشيع لعلي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه والغلو فيه، فلما بلغه ذلك أنكره وحرَّق بالنار جماعة منهم ممن غلا فيه...، ثم حدث بعد عصر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهب جهم بن صفوان ببلاد المشرق، فعظمت الفتنة به، فإنه نفى أن يكون الله تعالى صفة، وأورد على أهل الإسلام شكوكًا أثرت في الملة الإسلامية آثارًا قبيحة تولد عنها بلاء كبير، وكان قبل المئة من سني الهجرة، فكثر أتباعه على أقواله التي تؤول إلى التعطيل .... وفي أثناء ذلك حدث مذهب الاعتزال منذ زمن الحسن بن الحسين البصري رحمه الله بعد المئتين من سني الهجرة، وألفوا في مسائل العدل والتوحيد وإثبات أفعال العباد وأن الله تعالى لا يخلق الشر، وجهروا بأن الله لا يُرى في الآخرة، وأنكروا عذاب القبر على البدن، وأعلنوا بأن القرءان مخلوق محدث، إلى غير ذلك من مسائلهم، فتبعهم خلائق في بدعهم.... ثم حدث مذهب التجسيم المضاد لمذهب الاعتزال، فظهر محمد بن كرام بن عراق السجستاني زعيم الطائفة الكرامية... وأثبت الصفات حتى انتهى فيها إلى التجسيم والتشبيه...» إلى آخر كلامه رحمه الله.
طائفة بعد مناظرة عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما لهم. (1) أي جميعهم، وإلا فقد رجع منهم
Then the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, passed in this manner until the discourse on al-Qadr (predestination) occurred in their time... And the first to speak about al-Qadr was Ma'bad ibn Khalid al-Juhani... And when 'Abdullah ibn al-Khattab heard Ma'bad's statement about al-Qadr, he disassociated himself from the Qadariyya... And also during the time of Ibn 'Umar, among the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of the Khawarij emerged, and they openly declared takfir (excommunication) for sin and rebellion against the Imam and fighting him. So 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, debated them, but they did not return to the truth (1), and the Commander of the Faithful, 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, fought them and killed a group of them, as is well-known... And during the time of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of al-Tashayyu' (partisanship) for 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, and extremism in it emerged. When he heard of that, he denounced it and burned a group of them who had exaggerated in it... Then after the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of Jahm ibn Safwan emerged in the lands of the East, and a great tribulation arose because of him. He denied that Allah Almighty has attributes, and he presented doubts to the people of Islam that had ugly effects on the Islamic community, from which a great calamity was born. This was before one hundred years of the Hijra, and his followers multiplied on his statements that lead to ta'til (divesting God of attributes)... And during that, the sect of al-I'tizal emerged from the time of al-Hasan ibn al-Husayn al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him, after two hundred years of the Hijra. They wrote on issues of justice, tawhid (unity of God), affirmation of the actions of servants, and that Allah Almighty does not create evil. They openly stated that Allah will not be seen in the Hereafter, denied the punishment of the grave upon the body, and declared that the Qur'an is created and originated, along with other issues of theirs. So multitudes followed them in their innovations... Then the sect of al-Tajsim (anthropomorphism), opposed to the Mu'tazilite sect, emerged. So Muhammad ibn Karram ibn 'Iraq al-Sijistani appeared, the leader of the Karramiyya sect... He affirmed the attributes until he ended up in tajsim (corporealism) and tashbih (anthropomorphism)... End of his speech, may Allah have mercy on him.
A group after the debate of 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, with them. (1) meaning all of them, otherwise some of them did return.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative detailing the emergence of various theological sects in early Islam. The author begins by noting that the era of the Companions continued peacefully until the advent of discourse on al-Qadr (predestination) with Ma'bad al-Juhani. When Umar ibn al-Khattab heard this, he disassociated from the Qadariyya. Then the Khawarij arose, advocating takfir for major sins and rebellion; Abdullah ibn Abbas debated them, and Ali fought them. Subsequently, the Shi'a emerged with extremism, which Ali himself denounced and punished. After the Companions' era, Jahm ibn Safwan appeared in the East, denying attributes and causing great fitna. Then the Mu'tazila arose around Hasan al-Basri's school, advocating justice, denying vision of Allah, and claiming the Qur'an is created. In opposition, the Karramiyya emerged, affirming attributes excessively to the point of anthropomorphism. The author concludes by quoting another source, and a footnote clarifies that a group returned after Ibn Abbas's debate. This overview sets the stage for why kalam became necessary: to defend orthodox belief against these deviations.

Key Terms
القدرية
القدر
معبد بن خالد الجهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who was the first to speak about al-Qadr according to the passage?
A) Ma'bad al-Juhani
B) Umar ibn al-Khattab
C) Jahm ibn Safwan
D) Hasan al-Basri

Key Takeaway
The first sectarian deviation in Islam was the denial of predestination, initiated by Ma'bad al-Juhani and immediately rejected by the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone deny that God controls all affairs, do you feel the same urgency as Umar ibn al-Khattab to explicitly disassociate from that belief, or do you remain silent to avoid tension? What would it cost you to speak the truth clearly?
?
Ma'bad al-Juhani was among the Muslims, yet his error began with a single idea about free will. Are there any subtle beliefs you hold that elevate your own ability over God's decree? Ask yourself: do you truly submit to God's plan even when it contradicts your desires?
?
The Companions lived in unity until one man's statement about al-qadr sparked division. How quickly do you adopt or spread new ideas about religion without measuring them against the example of the Prophet's companions? Will you commit to verifying every opinion against their consensus?

The Emergence of the Khawarij
Khawarij beliefs and the Companions' response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the emergence of the Khawarij sect and the Companions' response to their extremism.

Core Idea
The Khawarij introduced takfir for major sins and rebellion against the Imam.

The passage moves to the Khawarij, who arose during the time of the Companions. They declared that major sins remove a person from Islam, justifying rebellion against the legitimate ruler. The Companions debated and fought them, as recorded here.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the text about the Khawarij, including the footnote.
The footnote clarifies that some returned from their error.
2
Identify their beliefs
They believed in takfir for sin and rebellion against the Imam.
3
Learn the response
Abdullah ibn Abbas debated them, and Ali fought them.
The footnote says some returned after the debate.

Source Text

طائفة بعد مناظرة عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما لهم. (1) أي جميعهم، وإلا فقد رجع منهم
Then the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, passed in this manner until the discourse on al-Qadr (predestination) occurred in their time... And the first to speak about al-Qadr was Ma'bad ibn Khalid al-Juhani... And when 'Abdullah ibn al-Khattab heard Ma'bad's statement about al-Qadr, he disassociated himself from the Qadariyya... And also during the time of Ibn 'Umar, among the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of the Khawarij emerged, and they openly declared takfir (excommunication) for sin and rebellion against the Imam and fighting him. So 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, debated them, but they did not return to the truth (1), and the Commander of the Faithful, 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, fought them and killed a group of them, as is well-known... And during the time of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of al-Tashayyu' (partisanship) for 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, and extremism in it emerged. When he heard of that, he denounced it and burned a group of them who had exaggerated in it... Then after the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of Jahm ibn Safwan emerged in the lands of the East, and a great tribulation arose because of him. He denied that Allah Almighty has attributes, and he presented doubts to the people of Islam that had ugly effects on the Islamic community, from which a great calamity was born. This was before one hundred years of the Hijra, and his followers multiplied on his statements that lead to ta'til (divesting God of attributes)... And during that, the sect of al-I'tizal emerged from the time of al-Hasan ibn al-Husayn al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him, after two hundred years of the Hijra. They wrote on issues of justice, tawhid (unity of God), affirmation of the actions of servants, and that Allah Almighty does not create evil. They openly stated that Allah will not be seen in the Hereafter, denied the punishment of the grave upon the body, and declared that the Qur'an is created and originated, along with other issues of theirs. So multitudes followed them in their innovations... Then the sect of al-Tajsim (anthropomorphism), opposed to the Mu'tazilite sect, emerged. So Muhammad ibn Karram ibn 'Iraq al-Sijistani appeared, the leader of the Karramiyya sect... He affirmed the attributes until he ended up in tajsim (corporealism) and tashbih (anthropomorphism)... End of his speech, may Allah have mercy on him.
A group after the debate of 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, with them. (1) meaning all of them, otherwise some of them did return.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Khawarij were killed and none repented.
Correction: The footnote indicates that some returned from their error after the debate with Ibn Abbas.

Checkpoint
Who fought the Khawarij according to the passage?
A) Umar ibn al-Khattab
B) Ali ibn Abi Talib
C) Abdullah ibn Abbas
D) Ma'bad al-Juhani

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij introduced takfir and rebellion; they were debated by Ibn Abbas and fought by Ali, but some repented.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a sin, do you ever entertain the thought that they might no longer be a believer? How does the Khawarij’s error of takfir warn you about the danger of judging others’ faith based on their actions?
?
Have you ever felt justified in disobeying a parent, teacher, or leader because you believed they were wrong or unjust? What does this lesson teach you about the fine line between righteous objection and sinful rebellion?
?
The Khawarij were utterly convinced of their correctness, yet they were deeply mistaken. How can you protect yourself from a similar certainty in your own views, especially when they lead you to break away from the community of Muslims?

The Emergence of the Shi'a
Shi'a origins and Ali's rejection of extremism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early partisanship for Ali and how he himself opposed extremism.

Core Idea
The Shi'a began as a partisan group for Ali, but Ali denounced their extremism.

The passage mentions the emergence of tashayyu' (partisanship) for Ali during the Companions' era. Some people exaggerated in their love for Ali, elevating him beyond his status. Ali himself rejected this and took harsh measures against them. This lesson covers that early phase.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text about the Shi'a emergence.
Notice the phrase 'al-ghulu' (extremism).
2
Understand the response of Ali
Ali denounced and punished the extremists by burning.
This shows that the leader of the group rejected the extreme followers.
3
Differentiate from later Shi'a
This early group was a small extremist faction, not mainstream Shi'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمضى عصر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم على هذا إلى أن حدث في زمنهم القول بالقدر.... وكان أول من قال بالقدر معبد بن خالد الجهني.... ولما بلغ عبد الله الخطاب مقالة معبد في القدر تبرأ من القدرية...، وحدث أيضًا في زمن ابن عمر بن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهب الخوارج، وصرَّحوا بالتكفير بالذنب والخروج على الإمام وقتاله، فناظرهم عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما فلم يرجعوا إلى الحق (۱) وقاتلهم أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه وقتل منهم جماعة كما هو معروف.... وحدث في زمن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهبُ التشيع لعلي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه والغلو فيه، فلما بلغه ذلك أنكره وحرَّق بالنار جماعة منهم ممن غلا فيه...، ثم حدث بعد عصر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهب جهم بن صفوان ببلاد المشرق، فعظمت الفتنة به، فإنه نفى أن يكون الله تعالى صفة، وأورد على أهل الإسلام شكوكًا أثرت في الملة الإسلامية آثارًا قبيحة تولد عنها بلاء كبير، وكان قبل المئة من سني الهجرة، فكثر أتباعه على أقواله التي تؤول إلى التعطيل .... وفي أثناء ذلك حدث مذهب الاعتزال منذ زمن الحسن بن الحسين البصري رحمه الله بعد المئتين من سني الهجرة، وألفوا في مسائل العدل والتوحيد وإثبات أفعال العباد وأن الله تعالى لا يخلق الشر، وجهروا بأن الله لا يُرى في الآخرة، وأنكروا عذاب القبر على البدن، وأعلنوا بأن القرءان مخلوق محدث، إلى غير ذلك من مسائلهم، فتبعهم خلائق في بدعهم.... ثم حدث مذهب التجسيم المضاد لمذهب الاعتزال، فظهر محمد بن كرام بن عراق السجستاني زعيم الطائفة الكرامية... وأثبت الصفات حتى انتهى فيها إلى التجسيم والتشبيه...» إلى آخر كلامه رحمه الله.
طائفة بعد مناظرة عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما لهم. (1) أي جميعهم، وإلا فقد رجع منهم
Then the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, passed in this manner until the discourse on al-Qadr (predestination) occurred in their time... And the first to speak about al-Qadr was Ma'bad ibn Khalid al-Juhani... And when 'Abdullah ibn al-Khattab heard Ma'bad's statement about al-Qadr, he disassociated himself from the Qadariyya... And also during the time of Ibn 'Umar, among the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of the Khawarij emerged, and they openly declared takfir (excommunication) for sin and rebellion against the Imam and fighting him. So 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, debated them, but they did not return to the truth (1), and the Commander of the Faithful, 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, fought them and killed a group of them, as is well-known... And during the time of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of al-Tashayyu' (partisanship) for 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, and extremism in it emerged. When he heard of that, he denounced it and burned a group of them who had exaggerated in it... Then after the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of Jahm ibn Safwan emerged in the lands of the East, and a great tribulation arose because of him. He denied that Allah Almighty has attributes, and he presented doubts to the people of Islam that had ugly effects on the Islamic community, from which a great calamity was born. This was before one hundred years of the Hijra, and his followers multiplied on his statements that lead to ta'til (divesting God of attributes)... And during that, the sect of al-I'tizal emerged from the time of al-Hasan ibn al-Husayn al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him, after two hundred years of the Hijra. They wrote on issues of justice, tawhid (unity of God), affirmation of the actions of servants, and that Allah Almighty does not create evil. They openly stated that Allah will not be seen in the Hereafter, denied the punishment of the grave upon the body, and declared that the Qur'an is created and originated, along with other issues of theirs. So multitudes followed them in their innovations... Then the sect of al-Tajsim (anthropomorphism), opposed to the Mu'tazilite sect, emerged. So Muhammad ibn Karram ibn 'Iraq al-Sijistani appeared, the leader of the Karramiyya sect... He affirmed the attributes until he ended up in tajsim (corporealism) and tashbih (anthropomorphism)... End of his speech, may Allah have mercy on him.
A group after the debate of 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, with them. (1) meaning all of them, otherwise some of them did return.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative detailing the emergence of various theological sects in early Islam. The author begins by noting that the era of the Companions continued peacefully until the advent of discourse on al-Qadr (predestination) with Ma'bad al-Juhani. When Umar ibn al-Khattab heard this, he disassociated from the Qadariyya. Then the Khawarij arose, advocating takfir for major sins and rebellion; Abdullah ibn Abbas debated them, and Ali fought them. Subsequently, the Shi'a emerged with extremism, which Ali himself denounced and punished. After the Companions' era, Jahm ibn Safwan appeared in the East, denying attributes and causing great fitna. Then the Mu'tazila arose around Hasan al-Basri's school, advocating justice, denying vision of Allah, and claiming the Qur'an is created. In opposition, the Karramiyya emerged, affirming attributes excessively to the point of anthropomorphism. The author concludes by quoting another source, and a footnote clarifies that a group returned after Ibn Abbas's debate. This overview sets the stage for why kalam became necessary: to defend orthodox belief against these deviations.

Key Terms
التشيع
الغلو
علي بن أبي طالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali encouraged the Shi'a to venerate him excessively.
Correction: The passage states that when Ali heard about the extremism, he denounced it and punished the perpetrators.

Checkpoint
How did Ali respond to the extremists according to the passage?
A) He embraced them
B) He debated them
C) He burned them with fire
D) He exiled them

Key Takeaway
Early partisanship for Ali was a deviation that Ali himself rejected, burning those who exaggerated in their veneration of him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself exaggerating in your love or admiration for a religious figure, teacher, or leader, to the point where you justify things they themselves would reject? How can you actively guard against this?
?
If Ali ibn Abi Talib himself—a figure so beloved and respected—burned those who over-glorified him, what does that demand of you when you feel tempted to elevate any human being beyond their due?
?
Are you part of any group or movement that holds extreme views about its leaders, even subtly? Will you have the courage to question that, as Ali did, and stand for the truth even if it means opposing your own group?

The Emergence of the Jahmiyya
Jahmiyya emergence and ta'til

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rise of the Jahmiyya sect and its denial of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Jahm ibn Safwan denied God's attributes, causing great tribulation.

After the Companions' era, a major innovator named Jahm ibn Safwan appeared in the East. He denied that Allah has any attributes, which fell into ta'til (divesting God of attributes). This lesson focuses on his emergence and the impact of his teachings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read about Jahm ibn Safwan and his doctrine.
The word 'ta'til' is key.
2
Understand the consequences
His doubts caused great tribulation in the Muslim community.
The passage mentions 'fitna' and 'balā'.
3
Place the timeline
This occurred just before 100 AH.

Checkpoint
What was Jahm ibn Safwan's primary theological error?
A) He denied predestination
B) He denied Allah's attributes
C) He claimed the Qur'an is created
D) He said Allah is a body

Key Takeaway
Jahm ibn Safwan's denial of divine attributes (ta'til) caused a great fitna and led many astray.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that denying Allah's attributes led to a great fitna. Reflect: have you ever felt uncomfortable with any of Allah's attributes, perhaps thinking they conflict with His majesty? Where did that doubt come from? Make a firm intention today to study the names and attributes of Allah from trustworthy sources.
?
The passage says Jahm presented 'doubts' that had 'ugly effects.' Are you aware of any doubts about Allah that you have picked up from your environment, social media, or unreliable teachers? What specific step will you take to purge such doubts and ground yourself in authentic aqeedah?
?
His followers multiplied despite the calamity. Consider: whom do you follow in matters of faith? Have you ever uncritically accepted a belief because it sounded rational or came from a charismatic speaker? Commit to verifying every teaching about Allah with the Qur'an, Sunnah, and the understanding of the Salaf.

4 min
The Emergence of the Mu'tazila
Mu'tazila doctrines and spread

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the emergence of the Mu'tazila and their key doctrines.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila promoted rationalist doctrines like justice and the created Qur'an.

During the same period as the Jahmiyya, another sect arose: the Mu'tazila, associated with Hasan al-Basri. They wrote extensively on justice, tawhid, human actions, and denied the vision of Allah, punishment of the grave, and declared the Qur'an created. This lesson outlines their core beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the list of Mu'tazilite doctrines.
Note that they denied seeing Allah and the punishment of the grave.
2
Recognize their influence
They had many followers and produced many writings.
The passage says 'multitudes followed them.'
3
Connect to later debates
These issues became central in kalam discussions.

Source Text

This overview sets the stage for why kalam became necessary: to defend orthodox belief against these deviations.

The Mu'tazila promoted rationalist doctrines including the created Qur'an, denial of divine vision, and the idea that God does not create evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Mu'tazila denied the vision of Allah in the Hereafter, does your heart ache with longing to see your Lord? Or do you find that a rational denial suits you more comfortably? Commit to asking Allah for the blessing of seeing Him.
?
The Mu'tazila said that God does not create evil. Look at your own life: when hardship strikes, do you first blame God in your heart, or do you trust His wisdom? What does your reaction reveal about your belief in divine decree? Take a moment to repent if needed.
?
The Mu'tazila boldly declared that the Qur'an is created. Do you treat the Qur'an as a mere book of guidance that you can take or leave, or as the eternal speech of Allah that demands your obedience? How can you elevate your reverence for Allah's speech today?

The Emergence of the Karramiyya
Karramiyya anthropomorphism as anti-Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya sect's exaggeration in affirming God's attributes to the point of anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya overcorrected against the Mu'tazila by falling into anthropomorphism.

In opposition to the Mu'tazila, who denied attributes, the Karramiyya emerged, led by Muhammad ibn Karram. They affirmed Allah's attributes so excessively that they ended up with corporealism and anthropomorphism. This lesson covers that reactionary movement.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage about the Karramiyya.
The terms 'tajsim' and 'tashbih' are key.
2
Understand the context
They arose as a reaction to the Mu'tazila.
The passage says 'opposed to the Mu'tazilite sect.'
3
Recognize the error
They affirmed attributes so much that they fell into anthropomorphism.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمضى عصر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم على هذا إلى أن حدث في زمنهم القول بالقدر.... وكان أول من قال بالقدر معبد بن خالد الجهني.... ولما بلغ عبد الله الخطاب مقالة معبد في القدر تبرأ من القدرية...، وحدث أيضًا في زمن ابن عمر بن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهب الخوارج، وصرَّحوا بالتكفير بالذنب والخروج على الإمام وقتاله، فناظرهم عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما فلم يرجعوا إلى الحق (۱) وقاتلهم أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه وقتل منهم جماعة كما هو معروف.... وحدث في زمن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهبُ التشيع لعلي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه والغلو فيه، فلما بلغه ذلك أنكره وحرَّق بالنار جماعة منهم ممن غلا فيه...، ثم حدث بعد عصر الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مذهب جهم بن صفوان ببلاد المشرق، فعظمت الفتنة به، فإنه نفى أن يكون الله تعالى صفة، وأورد على أهل الإسلام شكوكًا أثرت في الملة الإسلامية آثارًا قبيحة تولد عنها بلاء كبير، وكان قبل المئة من سني الهجرة، فكثر أتباعه على أقواله التي تؤول إلى التعطيل .... وفي أثناء ذلك حدث مذهب الاعتزال منذ زمن الحسن بن الحسين البصري رحمه الله بعد المئتين من سني الهجرة، وألفوا في مسائل العدل والتوحيد وإثبات أفعال العباد وأن الله تعالى لا يخلق الشر، وجهروا بأن الله لا يُرى في الآخرة، وأنكروا عذاب القبر على البدن، وأعلنوا بأن القرءان مخلوق محدث، إلى غير ذلك من مسائلهم، فتبعهم خلائق في بدعهم.... ثم حدث مذهب التجسيم المضاد لمذهب الاعتزال، فظهر محمد بن كرام بن عراق السجستاني زعيم الطائفة الكرامية... وأثبت الصفات حتى انتهى فيها إلى التجسيم والتشبيه...» إلى آخر كلامه رحمه الله.
طائفة بعد مناظرة عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما لهم. (1) أي جميعهم، وإلا فقد رجع منهم
Then the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, passed in this manner until the discourse on al-Qadr (predestination) occurred in their time... And the first to speak about al-Qadr was Ma'bad ibn Khalid al-Juhani... And when 'Abdullah ibn al-Khattab heard Ma'bad's statement about al-Qadr, he disassociated himself from the Qadariyya... And also during the time of Ibn 'Umar, among the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of the Khawarij emerged, and they openly declared takfir (excommunication) for sin and rebellion against the Imam and fighting him. So 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, debated them, but they did not return to the truth (1), and the Commander of the Faithful, 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, fought them and killed a group of them, as is well-known... And during the time of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of al-Tashayyu' (partisanship) for 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, and extremism in it emerged. When he heard of that, he denounced it and burned a group of them who had exaggerated in it... Then after the era of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, the sect of Jahm ibn Safwan emerged in the lands of the East, and a great tribulation arose because of him. He denied that Allah Almighty has attributes, and he presented doubts to the people of Islam that had ugly effects on the Islamic community, from which a great calamity was born. This was before one hundred years of the Hijra, and his followers multiplied on his statements that lead to ta'til (divesting God of attributes)... And during that, the sect of al-I'tizal emerged from the time of al-Hasan ibn al-Husayn al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him, after two hundred years of the Hijra. They wrote on issues of justice, tawhid (unity of God), affirmation of the actions of servants, and that Allah Almighty does not create evil. They openly stated that Allah will not be seen in the Hereafter, denied the punishment of the grave upon the body, and declared that the Qur'an is created and originated, along with other issues of theirs. So multitudes followed them in their innovations... Then the sect of al-Tajsim (anthropomorphism), opposed to the Mu'tazilite sect, emerged. So Muhammad ibn Karram ibn 'Iraq al-Sijistani appeared, the leader of the Karramiyya sect... He affirmed the attributes until he ended up in tajsim (corporealism) and tashbih (anthropomorphism)... End of his speech, may Allah have mercy on him.
A group after the debate of 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, with them. (1) meaning all of them, otherwise some of them did return.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative detailing the emergence of various theological sects in early Islam. The author begins by noting that the era of the Companions continued peacefully until the advent of discourse on al-Qadr (predestination) with Ma'bad al-Juhani. When Umar ibn al-Khattab heard this, he disassociated from the Qadariyya. Then the Khawarij arose, advocating takfir for major sins and rebellion; Abdullah ibn Abbas debated them, and Ali fought them. Subsequently, the Shi'a emerged with extremism, which Ali himself denounced and punished. After the Companions' era, Jahm ibn Safwan appeared in the East, denying attributes and causing great fitna. Then the Mu'tazila arose around Hasan al-Basri's school, advocating justice, denying vision of Allah, and claiming the Qur'an is created. In opposition, the Karramiyya emerged, affirming attributes excessively to the point of anthropomorphism. The author concludes by quoting another source, and a footnote clarifies that a group returned after Ibn Abbas's debate. This overview sets the stage for why kalam became necessary: to defend orthodox belief against these deviations.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya were the same as the Ash'ariyya in affirming attributes.
Correction: The Karramiyya went beyond affirmation to corporealism and anthropomorphism, which is rejected.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya overemphasized God's attributes to the point of anthropomorphism, as a reaction to the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specific attribute are you most prone to misunderstanding, and what action will you take today to learn the correct Sunni position?
?
The Karramiyya reacted against the Mu'tazila by going to the opposite extreme. Reflect on a time when you corrected a mistake in your own belief or practice but risked falling into another error. How can you avoid swinging from one extreme to another in your faith?
?
This lesson shows that both denying attributes and over-affirming them are deviations. How does your current understanding of Allah's attribute of 'rising over the Throne' (istiwa') avoid both tashbih and ta'til? Write down a concrete way you will remind yourself of the balance.

Refutation of Modern Doubts About Kalam
Nasafi refutes contemporary accusations that kalam is bid'ah and harmful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main modern accusations against ilm al-kalam and how Nasafi refutes them, including the misuse of Salaf statements.

Core Idea
Modern doubts about kalam are weaker than a spider's web.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses contemporary criticisms that label kalam as a blameworthy innovation and question its Islamic foundations. He identifies the key accusations, from being derived from philosophy to being prohibited, and then refutes them. The lesson will guide you through his refutation and the proper understanding of Salaf's warnings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

The passage shows the sectarian dimension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفع شبهات حول علم الكلام تجددت في العصر الحديث دعوات تُلحِقُ علم الكلام بالبدع المنكرة، وأن الاشتغال به حرام لأنه مبني على غير الكتاب والسنة، وأن استمداده من آراء الفلاسفة والوثنيين، وأن الاكتفاء بالكتاب والسنة هو الحصن لطالبي العقيدة الصحيحة. ومن ثَمَّ رُمِيَ أهل السنة والجماعة من الأشاعرة والماتريدية بأنهم أهل التعطيل أو أهل التأويل المذموم أو أنهم جهمية، إلى غير ذلك من ألقاب، في محاولة لصرف المسلمين عامة وطلبة العلم خاصةً عن مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، وتعلقت هذه الدعوات بشبهات أوْهَى من بيت العنكبوت. يحاولون أن يصوروا لنا علم الكلام كأنه من العلوم المذمومة التي لا تحل دراستها، ولا تحل القراءة فيها، وأن الاكتفاء بنصوص الكتاب والسنة المجردة هي الأصل الأصيل الذي لا بديل عنه ولا سبيل سواه. وينقلون عبارات للسلف الصالح فيها النهي النهي عن الخوض الكلام ويحملونها على علم الكلام الذي عرفناه سابقا، وللأسف فهذه طريقتهم دائما في تناول النصوص،
Refutation of Doubts about the Science of Kalam. In the modern era, calls have been renewed that attach the science of kalam to reprehensible innovations, and that engaging in it is forbidden because it is based on other than the Book and the Sunnah, and that its derivation is from the opinions of philosophers and pagans, and that sufficing with the Book and the Sunnah is the fortress for seekers of correct creed. Consequently, Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah among the Ash'aris and Maturidis have been accused of being people of ta'til (divesting God of attributes) or people of blameworthy ta'wil or that they are Jahmiyyah, among other labels, in an attempt to divert Muslims in general and students of knowledge in particular from the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah. And these calls have clung to doubts weaker than a spider's web. They try to depict for us the science of kalam as if it is among the blameworthy sciences whose study is not permissible, nor is reading in it permissible, and that sufficing with the bare texts of the Book and the Sunnah is the authentic principle with no alternative and no path besides it. And they transmit statements from the early Salaf containing prohibition, prohibition, about engaging in kalam, and they apply them to the science of kalam as we have defined it previously. Unfortunately, this is their constant method in handling texts.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of modern criticisms leveled against ilm al-kalam. Nasafi identifies the main accusations: that kalam is a blameworthy innovation, that studying it is forbidden, that it is based on Greek philosophy and pagan ideas, and that the Quran and Sunnah alone suffice. He then notes that these accusations lead to branding Ash'aris and Maturidis as people of ta'til (denial of attributes), blameworthy interpretation, or as Jahmiyyah. Nasafi dismisses these doubts as weaker than a spider's web. He points out that critics try to portray kalam as a prohibited science and that the only correct approach is to follow the bare texts. They also misuse statements of the Salaf that warned against engaging in kalam, applying them to the legitimate science of kalam as defined earlier. Nasafi concludes by lamenting that this is their ongoing method in dealing with texts—taking them out of context.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That early Salaf's warnings against kalam apply to all forms of kalam
Correction: Nasafi argues they refer to illegitimate speculation, not the science of kalam as defined by Ahl al-Sunnah.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes modern doubts by affirming that kalam is rooted in Quran and Sunnah, and that critics misuse Salaf statements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The critics misuse statements of the Salaf. Are there any Islamic teachings you have misinterpreted or taken out of context to support your own biases? How can you ensure you approach texts with the same care that Nasafi demands?

The Two Wings of Ahl al-Sunna
The author names the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools, their founders, and their dominance among Hanafis and Shafi'is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the origins and historical affiliation of the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools as the two main theological schools of Sunni Islam.

Core Idea
Maturidis and Ash'aris are the two wings of Ahl al-Sunna.

The author introduces the two great Sunni theological schools by naming their founders and explaining their historical association with the Hanafi and Shafi'i legal schools. This lesson establishes the foundational identity of these schools as the two wings of Sunni orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage introducing the schools
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author asserts that these two schools form the core of Sunni theology.
2
Identify the founders and their dates
Note the names and death dates of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (333 AH) and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (324 AH). These are key reference points.
The closeness of their death dates indicates they were contemporaries.
3
Reflect on the historical correlation with legal schools
Consider why Maturidism became dominant among Hanafis and Ash'arism among Shafi'is. The author hints at historical and political reasons.
This correlation is a well-known historical fact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين الماتريدية والأشعرية:
المذهب الماتريدي نسبة إلى الإمام الزاهد أبي منصور الماتريدي المتوفى ۳۳۳ هجرية، والمذهب الأشعري نسبة إلى الإمام أبي الحسن الأشعري المتوفى ٣٢٤ هجرية، ولا يخفى أن هذين المذهبين هما جناحا أهل السنة والجماعة، وهذا أمرٌ لا سبيل إلى مصادرته أو التشكيك فيه، لأن الحقائق التاريخية قبل الحقائق الدينية والكلامية تؤكد ذلك، فقلما وُجِدَ عالم من علماء الإسلام إلا وتجده إما أن يكون ماتريديًا أو أشعريًا.
وغلبت الماتريدية على أصحاب المذهب الحنفي، وغلبت الأشعرية على أصحاب المذهب الشافعي، ولعل لذلك أسبابه التاريخية والسياسية، خاصة فيما يتعلق بالمذهب الأشعري
بدأ أبو الحسن الأشعري طريقه في العلم على يد أبي علي الجبائي إمام المعتزلة، فقضى دهرًا من عمره كمعتزلي يدرس الاعتزال، ويناظر من أجله، وينافح عنه، إلى أن تحول عن الاعتزال وسلك طريق أهل السنة والجماعة ، بل إنه ناظر المعتزلة وصنف في الرد عليهم، وأصبح إماما من أئمة أهل السنة.
هذه
ويشير كثير من أهل العلم إلى أن المسائل الخلافية التي بين الماتريدية والأشعرية مسائل يسيرة والخلاف في جُلّها لفظي، وقد صنف عدد من أهل العلم في حصر المسائل والكلام عليها منها: «الروضة البهية فيما بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية» للحسن بن عبد المحسن الشهير بأبي عذبة كان حيا ۱۱۷۲ هجرية، و مسائل الاختلاف بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية لابن كمال باشا وغيرهما.
ويقول الإمام البياضي: إن من يرى أن الخلاف بين الماتريدية والأشعرية لفظي فقط مخطى حيث يقول في إشارات المرام ما صورته
Between the Maturidis and Ash'aris:
The Maturidi school is attributed to the ascetic Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH), and the Ash'ari school is attributed to Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (d. 324 AH). It is well known that these two schools are the two wings of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. This is a fact that cannot be denied or doubted, because historical facts, before religious and theological ones, affirm it. Rarely does one find a scholar of Islam who is not either Maturidi or Ash'ari.
The Maturidis predominated among the followers of the Hanafi school, and the Ash'aris predominated among the followers of the Shafi'i school, and this probably has its historical and political reasons, especially regarding the Ash'ari school.
Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari began his path in knowledge under Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i, the imam of the Mu'tazila. He spent a considerable part of his life as a Mu'tazili, studying Mu'tazilism, debating for it, and defending it, until he turned away from Mu'tazilism and took the path of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. Indeed, he debated the Mu'tazila and authored works refuting them, and became an imam of Ahl al-Sunna.
Many scholars point out that the points of disagreement between the Maturidis and Ash'aris are few, and the differences are mostly verbal. A number of scholars have written works enumerating and discussing these issues, including: 'al-Rawda al-Bahiyya fima bayn al-Ash'ara wa al-Maturidiyya' by al-Hasan ibn 'Abd al-Muhsin, known as Abu 'Udba (alive in 1172 AH), and 'Masa'il al-Ikhtilaf bayn al-Ash'ara wa al-Maturidiyya' by Ibn Kamal Basha, among others.
Imam al-Bayadi says: 'Whoever thinks that the disagreement between the Maturidis and Ash'aris is purely verbal is mistaken,' as he says in Isharat al-Maram: ... (the rest is cut off).
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the two main Sunni theological schools. The author begins by naming the founders: Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH) and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (d. 324 AH). He asserts that these two schools are the two 'wings' of Ahl al-Sunna, and virtually all Sunni scholars belong to one or the other. He notes a historical correlation: Maturidism became dominant among Hanafis, and Ash'arism among Shafi'is, attributing this to historical and political factors. He then narrates al-Ash'ari's personal journey: he was a student of the Mu'tazili leader al-Jubba'i and was himself a Mu'tazili for a long time, but then he repudiated Mu'tazilism and became a defender of Sunni orthodoxy, even writing refutations. The passage then addresses the nature of disagreements between the two schools: many scholars say the disagreements are few and largely verbal. The author mentions two works that compiled these differences. Finally, he quotes Imam al-Bayadi, who counters the claim that all differences are purely verbal, stating such an opinion is mistaken; however, his full argument is cut off. This leaves the reader with an open-ended discussion.

Key Terms
الماتريدية
الأشعرية
أهل السنة والجماعة
مذهب حنفي
مذهب شافعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the author say about the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools?
A) They are the only two schools in Islam.
B) They are the two wings of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a.
C) They are both derived from the Mu'tazila.
D) The Ash'ari school is predominant among Hanafis.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi and Ash'ari schools are the two main Sunni theological schools, historically associated with the Hanafi and Shafi'i legal schools respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that nearly every Sunni scholar for centuries has been either Ash'ari or Maturidi. Is your own theological stance aligned with this mainstream? If not, are you willing to honestly examine the implications of standing outside the consensus? Commit to seeking understanding from within this tradition.
?
The author presents these two schools as wings—complementary, not competing. Do you carry any partisan attitude in your heart that favors one over the other? How can you actively work to embody the unity of Ahl al-Sunna, even while studying theological differences? Make a concrete resolution to avoid sectarian language.

The Conversion of Imam al-Ash'ari and the Nature of Differences
The author narrates al-Ash'ari's conversion and discusses the nature of Maturidi-Ash'ari disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the story of Imam al-Ash'ari's conversion from Mu'tazilism to Sunni orthodoxy and the scholarly debate about the extent of differences between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's personal journey from Mu'tazilism shows the dynamic nature of Islamic theology.

The author narrates the remarkable story of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who began as a Mu'tazili and later became a leading Sunni imam. Then he addresses the nature of disagreements between the two schools, noting that many deem them minor but Imam al-Bayadi disagrees.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of al-Ash'ari's conversion
Read the Arabic passage about al-Ash'ari's early life under al-Jubba'i and his eventual turn to Sunni Islam. Reflect on the significance of a former Mu'tazili becoming a Sunni leader.
2
Reflect on the significance of his conversion
Consider why al-Ash'ari's background matters: it shows that Sunni theology was formulated partly in response to Mu'tazilism.
Al-Ash'ari's works refuting the Mu'tazila are foundational.
3
Read the discussion about the nature of differences
The author mentions that many scholars consider Maturidi-Ash'ari differences to be few and largely verbal. Note the titles of works that compiled these differences.
The two works are 'al-Rawda al-Bahiyya' and 'Masa'il al-Ikhtilaf'.
4
Consider the quote from al-Bayadi
Imam al-Bayadi warns that it is a mistake to think all differences are purely verbal. The passage ends mid-sentence, leaving the discussion open.
This shows that the issue is not settled; there is nuance.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين الماتريدية والأشعرية:
المذهب الماتريدي نسبة إلى الإمام الزاهد أبي منصور الماتريدي المتوفى ۳۳۳ هجرية، والمذهب الأشعري نسبة إلى الإمام أبي الحسن الأشعري المتوفى ٣٢٤ هجرية، ولا يخفى أن هذين المذهبين هما جناحا أهل السنة والجماعة، وهذا أمرٌ لا سبيل إلى مصادرته أو التشكيك فيه، لأن الحقائق التاريخية قبل الحقائق الدينية والكلامية تؤكد ذلك، فقلما وُجِدَ عالم من علماء الإسلام إلا وتجده إما أن يكون ماتريديًا أو أشعريًا.
وغلبت الماتريدية على أصحاب المذهب الحنفي، وغلبت الأشعرية على أصحاب المذهب الشافعي، ولعل لذلك أسبابه التاريخية والسياسية، خاصة فيما يتعلق بالمذهب الأشعري
بدأ أبو الحسن الأشعري طريقه في العلم على يد أبي علي الجبائي إمام المعتزلة، فقضى دهرًا من عمره كمعتزلي يدرس الاعتزال، ويناظر من أجله، وينافح عنه، إلى أن تحول عن الاعتزال وسلك طريق أهل السنة والجماعة ، بل إنه ناظر المعتزلة وصنف في الرد عليهم، وأصبح إماما من أئمة أهل السنة.
هذه
ويشير كثير من أهل العلم إلى أن المسائل الخلافية التي بين الماتريدية والأشعرية مسائل يسيرة والخلاف في جُلّها لفظي، وقد صنف عدد من أهل العلم في حصر المسائل والكلام عليها منها: «الروضة البهية فيما بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية» للحسن بن عبد المحسن الشهير بأبي عذبة كان حيا ۱۱۷۲ هجرية، و مسائل الاختلاف بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية لابن كمال باشا وغيرهما.
ويقول الإمام البياضي: إن من يرى أن الخلاف بين الماتريدية والأشعرية لفظي فقط مخطى حيث يقول في إشارات المرام ما صورته
Between the Maturidis and Ash'aris:
The Maturidi school is attributed to the ascetic Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH), and the Ash'ari school is attributed to Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (d. 324 AH). It is well known that these two schools are the two wings of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. This is a fact that cannot be denied or doubted, because historical facts, before religious and theological ones, affirm it. Rarely does one find a scholar of Islam who is not either Maturidi or Ash'ari.
The Maturidis predominated among the followers of the Hanafi school, and the Ash'aris predominated among the followers of the Shafi'i school, and this probably has its historical and political reasons, especially regarding the Ash'ari school.
Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari began his path in knowledge under Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i, the imam of the Mu'tazila. He spent a considerable part of his life as a Mu'tazili, studying Mu'tazilism, debating for it, and defending it, until he turned away from Mu'tazilism and took the path of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. Indeed, he debated the Mu'tazila and authored works refuting them, and became an imam of Ahl al-Sunna.
Many scholars point out that the points of disagreement between the Maturidis and Ash'aris are few, and the differences are mostly verbal. A number of scholars have written works enumerating and discussing these issues, including: 'al-Rawda al-Bahiyya fima bayn al-Ash'ara wa al-Maturidiyya' by al-Hasan ibn 'Abd al-Muhsin, known as Abu 'Udba (alive in 1172 AH), and 'Masa'il al-Ikhtilaf bayn al-Ash'ara wa al-Maturidiyya' by Ibn Kamal Basha, among others.
Imam al-Bayadi says: 'Whoever thinks that the disagreement between the Maturidis and Ash'aris is purely verbal is mistaken,' as he says in Isharat al-Maram: ... (the rest is cut off).
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the two main Sunni theological schools. The author begins by naming the founders: Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH) and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (d. 324 AH). He asserts that these two schools are the two 'wings' of Ahl al-Sunna, and virtually all Sunni scholars belong to one or the other. He notes a historical correlation: Maturidism became dominant among Hanafis, and Ash'arism among Shafi'is, attributing this to historical and political factors. He then narrates al-Ash'ari's personal journey: he was a student of the Mu'tazili leader al-Jubba'i and was himself a Mu'tazili for a long time, but then he repudiated Mu'tazilism and became a defender of Sunni orthodoxy, even writing refutations. The passage then addresses the nature of disagreements between the two schools: many scholars say the disagreements are few and largely verbal. The author mentions two works that compiled these differences. Finally, he quotes Imam al-Bayadi, who counters the claim that all differences are purely verbal, stating such an opinion is mistaken; however, his full argument is cut off. This leaves the reader with an open-ended discussion.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
خلاف لفظي
أبو علي الجبائي
الروضة البهية
ابن كمال باشا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All differences between Maturidis and Ash'aris are purely verbal.
Correction: Imam al-Bayadi explicitly states that whoever thinks the disagreement is purely verbal is mistaken, as quoted in the passage.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Ash'ari do after leaving Mu'tazilism?
A) He joined the Maturidi school.
B) He debated the Mu'tazila and wrote refutations of them.
C) He abandoned theology altogether.
D) He became a follower of al-Jubba'i.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ash'ari converted from Mu'tazilism to Sunni Islam and became a major Sunni figure; while many scholars consider Maturidi-Ash'ari differences minor, some, like al-Bayadi, caution against reducing them to mere verbal disagreements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam al-Ash'ari devoted years to studying and defending a theology he later rejected. Have you ever held a belief or worldview that you later came to see as false? What made you change? Are you willing to re-examine your current convictions with the same honesty and courage?
?
The passage notes that many scholars consider Maturidi-Ash'ari differences minor, while al-Bayadi warns against dismissing them entirely. In your interactions with other Muslims, do you tend to minimize or exaggerate differences? How can you strike the right balance between unity and truth?
?
Al-Ash'ari didn't just leave Mu'tazilism; he actively refuted it. If you discover a mistake in your understanding of Islam, do you have the courage to openly correct it and help others? Or do you cling to past positions out of pride or fear?

The Two Wings of Ahl al-Sunnah: Maturidi and Ash'ari
Introduces the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools as the two wings of Ahl al-Sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools together constitute the theological backbone of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, and how they are historically linked to the Hanafi and Shafi'i legal schools.

Core Idea
Maturidis and Ash'aris are the two wings of Sunni orthodoxy.

Before diving into specific theological arguments, it is essential to recognize the two major schools that have shaped Sunni theology. The Maturidi and Ash'ari traditions are not opposing sects but complementary pillars of Ahl al-Sunnah. This lesson from Imam Nasafi establishes their foundational status and historical alignment with the Hanafi and Shafi'i madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the founders and their eras
Note the names and death dates of Imam al-Maturidi (333 AH) and Imam al-Ash'ari (324 AH). These are the eponymous founders.
Remember that both lived in the 4th century AH, a formative period for Sunni theology.
2
Grasp the 'two wings' metaphor
The author explicitly calls these two schools the 'two wings' of Ahl al-Sunnah. Reflect on why he uses this imagery—both are necessary for flight, neither can stand alone.
3
Link the schools to legal madhhabs
Observe the historical pattern: Maturidi theology dominates among Hanafis, Ash'ari theology among Shafi'is. Think about how legal and theological schools often align.
The author hints at historical and political reasons for this alignment.

The Maturidi school is attributed to the ascetic Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who died in 333 AH, and the Ash'ari school is attributed to Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who died in 324 AH. It is not hidden that these two schools are the two wings of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, and this is a matter that cannot be confiscated or doubted, because historical truths before religious and theological truths confirm that. Rarely is a scholar of Islam found except that you find him either Maturidi or Ash'ari. Maturidism dominated the followers of the Hanafi school, and Ash'arism dominated the followers of the Shafi'i school, and perhaps for that there are historical and political reasons, especially concerning the Ash'ari school.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage introduces the relationship between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools. It begins by naming each school after its eponymous founder: Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH) and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (d. 324 AH). The author then asserts that these two schools together constitute the two wings of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, a status that cannot be contested either historically or theologically. He then observes that almost every Islamic scholar belongs to one of these two schools. The passage concludes by noting the historical association: Maturidism is predominant among Hanafis, and Ash'arism among Shafi'is, attributing this to historical and political factors, especially regarding the Ash'ari school.

Correction: This passage clarifies that they are the two complementary wings of Ahl al-Sunnah, both equally orthodox, and together they encompass the vast majority of Sunni scholars.

Are Maturidi-Ash'ari Differences Merely Verbal?
Two contrasting views on the nature of Maturidi-Ash'ari disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly debate over whether theological differences between Maturidis and Ash'aris are verbal or substantive, and the implications for judging those differences.

Core Idea
Not all differences between schools are real; but some are meaningful, though not grounds for division.

This passage addresses a crucial question in Sunni theology: do the differences between Maturidis and Ash'aris amount to real disagreement or are they merely verbal? Scholars have debated this, and we will examine two key positions – the common view that most differences are minor and verbal, and the counterclaim that they are meaningful but not causes for anathematization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Get a sense of the two contrasting views.
Notice the author first states a common opinion, then presents a rebuttal.
2
Identify the two main views
The first view is that differences are few and mostly verbal. The second, from al-Bayadi, insists they are meaningful but in subsidiary matters.
Al-Bayadi explicitly rejects the 'merely verbal' characterization.
3
Examine the evidence cited
Note the works listed for the first view, and then the arguments and historical observation from al-Bayadi and al-Maqrizi.
Al-Maqrizi confirms there were about a dozen real differences that initially caused friction.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on why this distinction matters: it helps maintain Sunni unity by recognizing real but non-essential differences.
The passage suggests that these differences should not lead to charges of innovation (tabdi').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشير كثير من أهل العلم إلى أن المسائل الخلافية التي بين الماتريدية والأشعرية مسائل يسيرة والخلاف في جُلّها لفظي، وقد صنف عدد من أهل العلم في حصر المسائل والكلام عليها منها: «الروضة البهية فيما بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية» للحسن بن عبد المحسن الشهير بأبي عذبة كان حيا ١١٧٢ هجرية، و مسائل الاختلاف بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية لابن كمال باشا وغيرهما. ويقول الإمام البياضي: إن من يرى أن الخلاف بين الماتريدية والأشعرية لفظي فقط مخطى حيث يقول في إشارات المرام ما صورته وما قيل: إن معظم خلافه - يعني الأشعري - من الخلافيات اللفظية وهم، بل ،معنوي، ولكنه في التفاريع التي لا يجري في خلافها تبديع، ولأن الماتريدي مفصل لمذهب الإمام وأصحابه المظهرين قبل الأشعري لمذهب أهل السنة (١). ويُلْمِحُ المقريزي إلى طرف من هذا المعنى فيقول في «المواعظ والاعتبار»: «هذا وبين الأشاعرة والماتريدية أتباع أبي منصور محمد بن محمد بن محمود الماتريدي...، من الخلاف في العقائد ما هو مشهور في موضعه، وهو إذا تُتبع يبلغ بضع عشرة مسألة، كان بسببها في أول الأمر تباين وتنافر ، وقَدَحَ كل منهم في عقيدة الآخر إلا أن الأمر آل آخرًا إلى الإغضاء ولله الحمد» (۲).
Many scholars indicate that the disputed issues between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris are minor matters and the disagreement in most of them is verbal. Some scholars have compiled and discussed these issues, including: 'al-Rawda al-Bahiyya fima bayn al-Ashā'ira wa al-Māturīdiyya' by al-Hasan ibn 'Abd al-Muhsin, known as Abū 'Udhba (alive in 1172 AH), and 'Masā'il al-Ikhtilāf bayn al-Ashā'ira wa al-Māturīdiyya' by Ibn Kamāl Pāshā and others. Imam al-Bayāḍī says: 'Whoever thinks that the disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris is merely verbal is mistaken.' He states in 'Ishārāt al-Marām' as follows: 'What is said that most of his disagreement – meaning al-Ash'ari – is among the verbal differences is wrong, rather it is meaningful, but it is in branches where disagreement does not lead to declaring innovation, and because al-Māturīdī detailed the school of the Imam and his companions who manifested the school of Ahl al-Sunnah before al-Ash'ari.' (1) Al-Maqrīzī hints at part of this meaning in 'al-Mawā'iẓ wa al-I'tibār': 'Between the Ash'aris and the Maturidis, followers of Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Mahmud al-Maturidi... there is disagreement in beliefs that is well-known in its place, and if followed it reaches about ten issues. Initially this caused division and animosity, and each impugned the other's creed, but eventually the matter settled on overlooking, praise be to God.' (2)
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature of theological differences between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools. It begins by stating the common view that these differences are few and mostly verbal. It then cites two works that catalog these differences. The author then presents the contrary view of Imam al-Bayāḍī, who denies that they are merely verbal. Al-Bayāḍī insists that the differences are meaningful, though they occur in subsidiary matters (furū') where disagreement does not entail charges of innovation (tabdī'). He also notes that al-Māturīdī preceded al-Ash'ari in elaborating the Sunni position. Finally, al-Maqrīzī is quoted to confirm that there are real differences (about a dozen) that initially caused friction but were later tolerated.

Key Terms
الخلاف اللفظي
الخلاف المعنوي
التبديع
أبو عذبة
ابن كمال باشا
البياضي
المقريزي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All differences between Maturidis and Ash'aris are purely verbal and therefore irrelevant.
Correction: Al-Bayāḍī corrects this: the differences are meaningful (ma'nawī) but occur in subsidiary matters and do not justify tabdī'.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Bayāḍī, what is the nature of the differences between Maturidis and Ash'aris?
A) They are merely verbal
B) They are meaningful but in subsidiary matters
C) They are fundamental and require mutual anathema
D) They are all about historical facts

Key Takeaway
The differences between Maturidis and Ash'aris are considered by scholars to be real but minor, occurring in subsidiary matters, and should not lead to sectarian animosity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter differences of opinion among scholars you respect, what is your instinctive reaction? Do you tend to dismiss them as 'merely verbal' or do you assume a deeper division? This lesson challenges both extremes—where do you personally lean, and what does that reveal about your view of the Sunni tradition?
?
Think of a disagreement you have had with someone in your religious community (even a small one). Did you allow it to create distance or hostility? This lesson shows that two of the largest Sunni theological schools, despite real differences, eventually chose to overlook them. Is there a disagreement in your life that you need to 'overlook' for the sake of unity, without compromising truth?
?
Imam al-Bayāḍī insists that the differences are 'meaningful' but also that they do not justify tabdī'. This requires holding two things together: recognizing genuine difference while refusing to condemn. Where in your own heart do you struggle to hold that balance—either by minimizing real differences for the sake of 'unity' or by condemning others for matters that are actually subsidiary?

The Real Nature of Ash'ari-Maturidi Differences
Imam Nasafi clarifies the nature of Ash'ari-Maturidi differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of differences between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools as explained by Imam Nasafi, including that they are real but limited to subsidiary matters and do not warrant declaring innovation.

Core Idea
Ash'ari-Maturidi differences are real, but they do not affect fundamentals.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Bahr al-Kalam addresses a common misconception about the relationship between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. Some consider their differences merely verbal, but the author clarifies that they are substantive, albeit confined to subsidiary issues where disagreement does not incur charges of innovation. He also highlights the historical precedence of Maturidi's systematic presentation of the Sunni creed before al-Ash'ari.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify the author's two main claims: (a) the differences are real, not merely verbal, and (b) they are only in subsidiary matters where disagreement does not lead to tabdi'. Also note the historical point about Maturidi's precedence.
The passage contains a refutation of a misconception and a historical remark.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does this matter for Sunni unity? How does this passage prevent extremism in judging theological differences?
This connects to the learning objective about the nature of the differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأشعري - من الخلافيات اللفظية وهم، بل معنوي، ولكنه في التفاريع التي لا يجري في خلافها تبديع، ولأن الماتريدي مفصل لمذهب الإمام وأصحابه المظهرين قبل الأشعري لمذهب أهل السنة (١).
Al-Ash'ari - some think it is among verbal disagreements, but it is real, but it is in the branches in whose disagreement innovation does not occur, and because al-Maturidi is the elaborator of the school of the Imam and his companions who made manifest the school of Ahl al-Sunna before al-Ash'ari.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of the differences between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. The author asserts that it is a mistake to consider these differences merely verbal (lafzi); they are real (ma'nawi). However, these differences are limited to subsidiary matters (tafarīʿ) in which disagreement does not lead to declaring someone an innovator (tabdīʿ). Additionally, the author clarifies that Imam al-Maturidi was the one who systematically elaborated the creed of the imam (Abu Hanifa) and his companions, who had already presented the school of Ahl al-Sunna before al-Ash'ari appeared. This establishes Maturidi's precedence in defending the Sunni creed.

Key Terms
الخلافيات اللفظية
تبديع
الماتريدي
الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all Ash'ari-Maturidi differences are merely verbal and have no substance.
Correction: The author says they are real (ma'nawi), but only in the branches (tafarīʿ), not in fundamentals.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how should the differences between Ash'ari and Maturidi be characterized?
A) They are purely verbal and have no substance.
B) They are real, but pertain to subsidiary matters that do not incur innovation.
C) They are fundamental and require declaring one side as innovators.
D) They are not real but merely historical.

Key Takeaway
The differences between Ash'ari and Maturidi are real but limited to subsidiary matters, and Maturidi preceded Ash'ari in systematically presenting the Sunni creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter disagreements among Muslims, are you quick to classify them as 'merely verbal' to avoid tension? How does this lesson call you to take real differences seriously without escalating them to accusations of innovation? Will you examine your own heart and identify one area where you have minimized or exaggerated a difference, and commit to a more balanced approach?
?
The author emphasizes that real differences in subsidiary matters do not warrant tabdīʿ. Is there a person or group you have mentally labeled as deviant over a secondary issue? What would it look like for you to retract that label and see them as a fellow Muslim with a valid difference, as this lesson teaches? Will you seek reconciliation or at least refrain from judgment?
?
Al-Māturīdī built upon the existing Sunni creed rather than claiming originality. How does this challenge your own desire for recognition or originality in your faith practice? Are you willing to humbly build on the work of those before you without needing to be the first? What is one way you can honor the legacy of earlier scholars instead of seeking your own prominence?

Maqrizi on Ash'ari-Maturidi Relations
Maqrizi describes initial conflict and eventual tolerance between Ash'aris and Maturidis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical arc of Ash'ari-Maturidi relations as described by al-Maqrizi, from initial conflict to eventual tolerance.

Core Idea
Even serious theological disagreements can be overcome by tolerance.

Al-Maqrizi, a renowned historian, provides a valuable historical perspective on the relationship between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. He notes that while their differences were numerous and initially caused mutual condemnation, the final outcome was peaceful tolerance. This passage reminds us that sectarian disagreements in Islamic theology did not always remain hostile.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maqrizi quotation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Maqrizi acknowledges both the initial conflict and the eventual resolution.
The phrase 'ighḍāʾ' (tolerance) is key.
2
Identify the two phases
The passage describes two phases: first 'tabāyun wa tanāfur' (divergence and discord), then 'ighḍāʾ' (tolerance). Notice the contrast.
3
Consider the number of issues
Maqrizi says the differences amount to about a dozen issues. Reflect on how this small number of doctrinal differences could cause such initial hostility.
This shows that quantity does not always determine severity.
4
Understand the lesson for us
This historical example teaches that theological disagreements within Ahl al-Sunnah can be managed with tolerance. It also shows the value of historical sources in understanding doctrinal development.
Maqrizi's work al-Mawa'iz wa'l-I'tibar is an important historical source.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُلْمِحُ المقريزي إلى طرف من هذا المعنى فيقول في «المواعظ والاعتبار»: «هذا وبين الأشاعرة والماتريدية أتباع أبي منصور محمد بن محمد بن محمود الماتريدي...، من الخلاف في العقائد ما هو مشهور في موضعه، وهو إذا تُتبع يبلغ بضع عشرة مسألة، كان بسببها في أول الأمر تباين وتنافر ، وقَدَحَ كل منهم في عقيدة الآخر إلا أن الأمر آل آخرًا إلى الإغضاء ولله الحمد» (٢).
And al-Maqrizi alludes to a part of this meaning and says in al-Mawa'iz wa'l-I'tibar: 'This, and between the Ash'aris and Maturidis, followers of Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Mahmud al-Maturidi..., there is in creeds what is well known in its place, and if it is followed it amounts to a dozen issues, because of which at first there was divergence and discord, and each of them disparaged the creed of the other, but in the end the matter settled on tolerance, praise be to God.'
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage quotes the historian al-Maqrizi to provide historical context for the Ash'ari-Maturidi differences. He acknowledges that there are well-known creedal differences between the two schools, amounting to about ten to twenty issues. In the early period, these differences caused mutual condemnation and discord. However, over time, the situation evolved into mutual tolerance (ighḍāʾ), which Maqrizi praises. This shows that despite initial hostility, the broader Sunni community eventually accepted both schools as valid expressions of orthodoxy.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maqrizi records that despite initial hostility over a dozen creedal issues, the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools eventually settled into mutual tolerance.

Maqrizi praises the eventual tolerance with 'al-ḥamdu lillāh'. Are there any theological disagreements in your own community that you have allowed to cause division? How can you actively work towards the 'ighḍāʾ' that Maqrizi celebrates?
