The Murji'ah Claim: No Commands or Prohibitions
Nasafi records the Murji'ah's claim of no divine commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme Murji'ah position that Allah created without imposing commands or prohibitions, and why this contradicts Sunni orthodoxy.

Core Idea
The Murji'ah claimed Allah left creation unguided, without any commands.

The Murji'ah were known for separating faith from actions, but some took it to an extreme. In this passage, Nasafi records their claim that Allah created people and then abandoned them without any divine commands or prohibitions. This radical view undermines the very purpose of religion and must be refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key terms: 'مرجئة', 'خلق', 'أمر', 'نهي'. This is the claim of a deviant sect.
The passage is short but packs a serious theological error.
2
Understand the claim
The Murji'ah said Allah created creatures and then 'left them loose' (سيبهم), meaning no commands or prohibitions were given. This implies religious obligations are not from Allah.
This is an extreme form of irjā' (postponement) that denies divine legislation.
3
Recognize the doctrinal problem
If there are no commands or prohibitions, then there is no purpose in creation, no prophets, no reward or punishment. Sunni Islam affirms that Allah did command and forbid through revelation.
The refutation, though missing here, would argue from revelation and reason that Allah indeed sent messengers with commands.

A section on refuting the Murji'ah and showing the corruption of their doctrine. The Murji'ah said: Allah the Exalted created the creatures and left them loose, He did not command them nor forbid them.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Murji'ah sect. Nasafi begins by stating their erroneous claim: that Allah created people and left them without any commands or prohibitions (i.e., no religious obligations). This implies that faith does not require works, as works are not commanded. The passage is incomplete in the source, and the Sunni counter-argument is not provided here. The Murji'ah are known for postponing judgment and separating faith from deeds, and this claim is a radical expression of that view. The subsequent lines, which likely include the refutation, are not available in this window.

Refuting the Murji'ah on the Nature of Divine Commands
Nasafi refutes the Murji'ah on divine commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the presence or absence of a threat of punishment distinguishes obligatory divine commands from merely recommendatory ones.

Core Idea
Not all divine commands are the same; the threat of punishment reveals obligation.

The Murji'ah argued that God's commands are only recommendations, not obligations, and that no punishment follows disobedience. They cited Quranic verses about eating and hunting as evidence. Nasafi refutes this by providing a clear criterion: commands that carry a threat of punishment are obligatory, as demonstrated by verses on prayer and zakat. This lesson presents the debate and the decisive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Murji'ah claim
Understand their position: commands in the Quran are mere forms, not real obligations, and they are only recommendatory. Note their proof texts.
The Murji'ah rely on verses like 'Eat and drink' and 'When you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt.'
2
Identify the refutation's criterion
Nasafi distinguishes commands by whether they are followed by a threat (wa'id) for leaving them. Those without threat are recommendatory; those with threat are obligatory.
He provides examples from prayer and zakat, which have explicit threats.
3
Examine the rational argument
Nasafi adds that it is inconsistent with divine wisdom to leave creation without binding commands, citing verses that affirm accountability.
This strengthens the refutation beyond textual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الرد على المرجئة وبيان فساد مذهبهم. قالت المرجئة: إن الله تعالى خلق الخلق وسيبهم، لم يأمرهم ولم ينههم، وما جاء في القرآن ذلك صورة الأمر لا حقيقة الأمر، وهو على الندب والاستحباب، فإن أحسن فله الثواب، وإن أساء فلا عقاب عليه كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَكُلُوا وَاشْرَبُوا﴾ [الأعراف: 31] وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَأَصْطَادُوا﴾ [المائدة: 2]. والجواب عنه أن نقول: كلُّ أمرٍ لم يعقبه الوعيد بتركه فهو على الندب والاستحباب كما قلتم، وكل أمر يعقبه الوعيد بتركه فهو على الحتم والإيجاب كما في الصلاة. قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَخَلَفَ مِنْ بَعْدِهِمْ خَلْفٌ أَضَاعُوا الصَّلَوةَ وَاتَّبَعُوا الشَّهَوَاتِ فَسَوْفَ يَلْقَوْنَ غَيَّا إِلَّا مَن تَابَ وَآمَنَ وَعَمِلَ صَالِحًا﴾ [مريم: 59-60] وكما في الزكاة قال الله تعالى: ﴿يَوْمَ يُحْمَى عَلَيْهَا فِي نَارِ جَهَنَّمَ فَتُكْوَى بِهَا جِبَاهُهُمْ وَجُنُوبُهُمْ وَظُهُورُهُمْ﴾ [التوبة: 35] ولأنه لا يَحسُنُ من حكمة الحكيم جل جلاله أن يخلق الخلق مهملين لم يأمرهم ولم ينههم كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿أَيَحْسَبُ الْإِنسَنُ أَن يُتْرَكَ سُدًى﴾ [القيامة: 36] وقوله تعالى: ﴿أَفَحَسِبْتُمْ أَنَّمَا خَلَقْنَاكُمْ عَبَثًا وَأَنَّكُمْ إِلَيْنَا لَا تُرْجَعُونَ﴾ [المؤمنون: 115].
Chapter on refuting the Murji'ah and showing the corruption of their doctrine. The Murji'ah said: Indeed God Almighty created the creation and abandoned them, He did not command them nor forbid them, and what came in the Quran of that is the form of command not the reality of command, and it is on the basis of recommendation and desirability, so if one does well he has reward, and if one does evil there is no punishment upon him, as God Almighty said: 'Eat and drink' (7:31) and likewise His saying: 'And when you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt' (5:2). The answer to it is that we say: Every command that is not followed by a threat for leaving it is on the basis of recommendation and desirability as you say, and every command that is followed by a threat for leaving it is on the basis of obligation and necessity as in prayer. God Almighty said: 'Then there succeeded after them successors who neglected prayer and followed desires, so they will meet evil, except those who repent and believe and do righteous deeds' (19:59-60). And as in zakat, God Almighty said: 'On the day it will be heated in the fire of Hell and with it their foreheads, flanks, and backs will be branded' (9:35). And because it is not fitting from the wisdom of the Wise, exalted is His majesty, that He create creation neglected, not commanding them nor forbidding them, as God Almighty said: 'Does man think that he will be left aimless?' (75:36) and His saying: 'Did you think that We created you in vain and that you would not be returned to Us?' (23:115).
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author first presents the Murji'ah claim: that God did not truly command or forbid; rather, the commands in the Quran are mere forms, not real obligations, and they are only recommendatory (nadb, istihbab). They cite verses like 'Eat and drink' and 'When you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt' as evidence that commands indicate permission, not obligation. The author then gives the refutation: the correct criterion is whether the command is accompanied by a threat of punishment (wa'id) for omission. Commands without such a threat are indeed recommendatory, but commands with a threat are obligatory (hatm, ijab). He provides examples: prayer and zakat are accompanied by explicit warnings in the Quran (verses from Maryam and Tawba). Finally, he adds a rational argument: it is inconsistent with divine wisdom (hikma) to leave creation without binding commands, citing verses from Qiyama and Mu'minun that affirm human accountability. Thus the Murji'ah position is refuted.

Key Terms
مرجئة
أمر
ندب
استحباب
حتم
إيجاب
وعيد
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine commands are either all obligatory or all permissive without distinction.
Correction: The author shows that the criterion is whether a threat of punishment accompanies the command.

Checkpoint
What criterion does the author use to distinguish an obligatory command from a recommendatory one?
A) The command being from God directly
B) The command being followed by a threat of punishment for omission
C) The command being in the Meccan surahs
D) The command being repeated multiple times

Key Takeaway
The presence of a threat of punishment in the Quran for neglecting a command indicates that the command is obligatory, not merely recommendatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though God's commands in the Quran are real obligations, or have you subtly adopted the Murji'ah mindset, treating them as mere suggestions? Which command have you been neglecting, rationalizing that the threat doesn't apply to you?

?
If God had left you to your own devices with no binding commands, would you have sought Him? The Murji'ah thought He abandoned creation; do you sometimes act as if He has, living carelessly without accountability? What does your daily routine say about your belief in His commands?

The Denial of Saints' Miracles and the Opponents' Argument
Opponents' denial and reinterpretation of Quranic miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of the Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Jahmiyya against the validity of saints' miracles, and how they reinterpret the miracles attributed to Mary.

Core Idea
The opponents argue that if saints had miracles, there would be no distinction between prophets and saints.

The passage begins by identifying three groups that deny the miracles of saints: the Mu'tazila, the Rafida, and the Jahmiyya. They affirm prophetic miracles but reject saintly ones, fearing that acknowledging saints' miracles would erase the unique status of prophethood. Let's read their reasoning directly from the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement of denial
Notice how the author names the three groups and states their position clearly.
The author curses them rhetorically.
2
Identify the opponents' main argument
Find the logical reasoning they present: if saints had miracles, prophetic miracles would become invalid and the distinction would vanish.
3
Examine how they reinterpret Quranic examples
Read how they explain the miracles of Mary as actually belonging to Jesus or Zechariah.
They refer to verses 19:25 and 3:38.
4
Reflect on the logic of their position
Consider the implications: by denying saints' miracles entirely, they must reinterpret clear Quranic events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات كرامات الأولياء. قالت المعتزلة والرافضة والجهمية لعنهم الله كرامات الأولياء باطلة، أما معجزات الأنبياء ثابتة صحيحة، واحتجوا وقالوا لو قلنا بأن كرامات الأولياء ثابتة لبطلت معجزات الأنبياء، ولا يكون فرقًا بين الأنبياء والأولياء. ويقولون ما تحتجون علينا من كرامات مريم في قوله تعالى: (وَهُزَى إِلَيْكِ يجدع النَّخْلَةِ تُسَقِط عَلَيْكِ رُطَبًا جَنِيّا ) [مريم: ٢٥] ذلك كرامة عيسى، وكذلك في قوله تعالى: كُلَّمَا دَخَلَ عَلَيْهَا زَكَرِيَّا الْمِحْرَابَ وَجَدَ عِندَهَا رِزْقًا ﴾ [آل عمران: ۳۸] فذلك كرامة زكريا. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة: كرامات الأولياء جائزة، وهي لا تقدح في معجزات الأنبياء. وها هنا ثلاث مراتب : معجزات الأنبياء، وكرامات الأولياء، ومخادعات الأعداء.
A section on establishing the miracles of saints (karamat al-awliya). The Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Jahmiyya—may God curse them—said: The miracles of saints are false. As for the miracles (mu'jizat) of the prophets, they are established and true. They argued, saying: If we said that the miracles of saints are established, then the miracles of prophets would be invalidated, and there would be no distinction between prophets and saints. And they say: What you cite against us from the miracles of Mary in His statement: 'And shake toward yourself the trunk of the palm tree; it will drop upon you fresh, ripe dates' [Maryam: 25]—that is a miracle of Jesus. Likewise in His statement: 'Whenever Zechariah entered upon her in the sanctuary, he found with her provision' [Al 'Imran: 38]—that is a miracle of Zechariah. And Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Jama'ah said: The miracles of saints are permissible, and they do not detract from the miracles of prophets. And here are three ranks: miracles of prophets, miracles of saints, and deceptions of enemies.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny the miracles (karamat) of saints. The author begins by stating the position of the Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Jahmiyya, who claim that saints' miracles are false while prophets' miracles are true. Their argument: if saints had miracles, prophets' miracles would be invalid because there would be no distinction between prophets and saints. They also interpret the miracles attributed to Mary (e.g., the palm tree and the provision) as miracles of Jesus or Zechariah, not of Mary herself. Ahl al-Sunnah responds: saints' miracles are permissible and do not diminish prophets' miracles. The author then classifies extraordinary events into three ranks: the miracles (mu'jizat) of prophets, the miracles (karamat) of saints, and the deceptions (mukhad'at) of enemies. This distinction preserves the superiority of prophethood while affirming that God can honor saints with wonders.

Key Terms
كرامات الأولياء (karamat al-awliya)
معجزات الأنبياء (mu'jizat al-anbiya)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
الرافضة (al-Rafida)
الجهمية (al-Jahmiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saints' miracles are impossible because they would diminish the uniqueness of prophetic miracles.
Correction: The passage records this belief from the opponents, but Sunni Islam affirms saints' miracles as a distinct, non-competing category.

Checkpoint

B) If saints had miracles, prophets' miracles would become invalid and there would be no difference between them.
C) Miracles of Mary are actually miracles of Jesus, so saints cannot have miracles.
D) The Quran does not mention any saints' miracles.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and others deny saints' miracles to protect the uniqueness of prophetic miracles, but they must reinterpret clear Quranic texts.

The Sunni Position: Three Ranks of Extraordinary Events
Sunni affirmation and threefold classification of extraordinary events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni affirmation of saints' miracles and the three-fold classification that distinguishes prophetic miracles, saintly miracles, and deceptions of enemies.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunnah affirms saints' miracles as a distinct rank that does not diminish the uniqueness of prophetic miracles.

In response to the deniers, Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Jama'ah asserts that saints' miracles are not only possible but also do not detract from prophetic miracles. The author then introduces a threefold classification that clarifies the different types of extraordinary events. This lesson focuses on that classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sunni response
Notice the concise rebuttal: saints' miracles are permissible (ja'izah) and do not detract from prophetic miracles.
2
Understand the principle
The key point is that saintly miracles are of a different rank and do not challenge prophetic miracles.
3
Learn the three ranks
Memorize the three categories: mu'jizat (prophets), karamat (saints), mukhad'at (enemies).
4
Compare the differences
Reflect on how each rank serves a different purpose and bears different implications.

Allah's Power Over Devilish Deceptions
States the Sunni position on devilish deceptions as acts of Allah's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Ahl al-Sunna, devils are transformed by Allah alone to deceive humans, not by their own power.

Core Idea
Devils transform only by Allah's will, not by their own power.

This short passage from Imam Nasafi's Bahr al-Kalam clarifies a key Sunni doctrine regarding the abilities of devils. It addresses a common misconception that devils can independently change their forms. Instead, it affirms that any deceptive act of a devil is ultimately brought about by Allah's permission and transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once to get the gist. Pay attention to the phrase 'يصرهم الله تعالى' (Allah transforms them).
2
Identify the theological point
What does this passage say about the power of devils? Who is the agent of transformation? Note that the devils are the object of the verb 'transforms'.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why is it important to affirm that Allah transforms devils? How does this relate to tawheed? This prevents attributing creative power to any being other than Allah.

Source Text

Refutation of the Mu'tazila on the Influence of Devils
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila denial of shaytanic influence, citing hadith and Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine regarding the influence of devils on humans and the proofs from hadith and Quran.

Core Idea
Devils influence humans both inwardly (whispering) and outwardly (beautifying sin).

This passage presents the debate between the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna on whether devils can influence humans. The Mu'tazila deny it, but Ahl al-Sunna affirm it with clear proofs from the Prophet's hadith about the devil flowing like blood and the Quranic verse about beautifying deeds. Let's examine the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Notice the compact style typical of kalam works.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage first states the Mu'tazila claim, then the Sunni claim. Mark where each begins.
The Mu'tazila deny both inward and outward influence.
3
Examine the inward proof
The inward proof is the hadith of the devil flowing like blood. Understand how this proves internal influence and how fasting restricts it.
The hadith also implies a remedy.
4
Examine the outward proof
The outward proof is the Quranic verse about the devil beautifying deeds. Note that it refers to outward actions.
This shows devils can influence external behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات عمل الشياطين على بني آدم وتسلطهم عليهم قالت المعتزلة: إن الشياطين ليس لهم عمل على بني آدم، ولا يمكنهم أن يوسوسوا، ونفس الإنسان توسوسهم وكذلك الجن، قالوا: ليس لهم عمل على بني آدم في الظاهر والباطن. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة لهم عمل على بني آدم في الظاهر والباطن. أما في الباطن لما يُروى عن النبي أنه قال: إن الشيطان يجري في بني آدم مجرى الدم فضيقوا الجارحة بالجوع والعطش فثبتَ أنَّ لهم ولايةً في الباطن، فيوسوسوا الإنسان ويدعوا إلى الشر. وأما في الظاهر: يُزين المعاصي في قلوب العباد لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَزَيَّنَ لَهُمُ الشَّيْطَانُ أَعْمَالَهُمْ﴾ [النمل: 24]
Chapter on Proving the Action of the Devils upon the Children of Adam and Their Control over Them. The Mu'tazila said: The devils have no action upon the Children of Adam, and they cannot whisper (waswasa). The human soul whispers to them, and likewise the jinn. They said: They have no action upon the Children of Adam in the outward and inward. And Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a said: They have action upon the Children of Adam in the outward and inward. As for the inward: because of what is narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Indeed the devil flows in the son of Adam like the flow of blood, so constrict the passages with hunger and thirst.' So it is established that they have authority (wilaya) in the inward, so they whisper to the human and call to evil. As for the outward: He beautifies disobedience (ma'asi) in the hearts of the servants, due to the saying of Allah Most High: 'And the devil beautified for them their deeds' [An-Naml 27:24].
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position regarding the influence of devils (shayatin) on humans. The Mu'tazila claimed that devils have no ability to act upon humans or whisper to them; rather, it is the human soul (nafs) or jinn that cause whisperings. Ahl al-Sunna affirm that devils have influence both inwardly and outwardly. For inward influence, they cite the hadith that the devil runs through the son of Adam like blood, and the remedy is to narrow the passages with hunger and thirst (i.e., fasting). This hadith proves the devil's internal influence, enabling them to whisper and call to evil. For outward influence, they cite the Quranic verse where the devil beautified the deeds of the polytheists. Thus, devils can influence humans in both domains. The structure is clear: statement of opposing view, statement of Sunni view, then two proofs.

Key Terms
الشياطين
الوسوسة
المعتزلة
أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the devil cannot influence a believer at all.
Correction: The hadith proves the devil flows like blood, so he can influence inwardly, but the believer can protect himself with hunger and thirst (fasting).

Key Takeaway

The Sunni Assertion: Devils Influence Humans Inwardly and Outwardly
Nasafi states the Mu'tazila denial and Sunni assertion with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunnah affirm that devils have both inward and outward influence over humans, and how they prove it from hadith and Quran.

Core Idea
Devils influence humans inwardly through whispering and outwardly by beautifying sins.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a key sectarian difference: the Mu'tazila deny that devils have any real influence over humans, attributing whispers to the self and jinn. Ahl al-Sunnah, however, affirm both inward and outward influence, grounding their position in prophetic hadith and Quranic verses. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the reasoning behind the Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute
Read the passage to see the contrast between Mu'tazila and Sunni positions on devil influence.
The Mu'tazila deny any action of devils on humans.
2
Examine the inward proof
Look at the hadith of the prophet that the devil flows like blood, and the remedy of hunger and thirst.
This establishes inward influence (whispering).
3
Examine the outward proof
Read the Quranic verse cited where the devil beautified deeds.
This shows outward influence by making sins attractive.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note that the passage refutes the Mu'tazila by presenting scriptural evidence for both types of influence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات عمل الشياطين على بني آدم وتسلطهم عليهم قالت المعتزلة: إن الشياطين ليس لهم عمل على بني آدم، ولا يمكنهم أن يوسوسوا، ونفس الإنسان توسوسهم وكذلك الجن، قالوا: ليس لهم عمل على بني آدم في الظاهر والباطن. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة لهم عمل على بني آدم في الظاهر والباطن. أما في الباطن لما يُروى عن النبي أنه قال: إن الشيطان يجري في بني آدم مجرى الدم فضيقوا الجارحة بالجوع والعطش فثبت أن لهم ولايةً في الباطن، فيوسوسوا الإنسان ويدعوا إلى الشر. وأما في الظاهر: يُزين المعاصي في قلوب العباد لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَزَيَّنَ لَهُمُ الشَّيْطَنُ أَعْمَلَهُمْ ﴾
Chapter on proving the action of devils upon humans and their domination over them. The Mu'tazila said: The devils have no action upon humans, nor can they whisper, and the human self (nafs) whispers to them, likewise the jinn. They said: They have no action upon humans outwardly or inwardly. And Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: They have action upon humans outwardly and inwardly. As for inwardly: because of what is narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Indeed the devil flows in humans like the flow of blood, so narrow the passageway (jawāriḥ) through hunger and thirst.' So it is established that they have authority (wilāyah) inwardly, so they whisper to humans and call to evil. As for outwardly: He beautifies sins in the hearts of servants, according to the saying of Allah Most High: 'And the devil beautified their deeds for them.'
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal definition and proof passage. It begins by stating the chapter title: proving the action and domination of devils over humans. The author then presents the Mu'tazila position, which denies that devils have any action over humans—neither outward nor inward influence; they claim that it is the human self (nafs) and jinn that whisper, not the devils. In contrast, Ahl al-Sunnah affirm that devils do have influence both inwardly and outwardly. For inward influence, the author provides a hadith: the devil flows in humans like blood, and the remedy is to narrow the passageways through hunger and thirst, which implies that the devil can access the inner self and whisper evil. This establishes the reality of their inward authority (wilāyah). For outward influence, the author cites the Quranic verse (Surah al-Naml 27:24) where the devil beautified the deeds of the people, showing that devils can tempt outwardly by making sin appear attractive. The passage thus refutes the Mu'tazila denial by grounding the Sunni position in scriptural evidence.

The Mu'tazilite Objection and Sunni Response on the Mi'raj
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite objection and gives the Sunni reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite rational objection against a physical Mi'raj and the Sunni response based on divine power.

Core Idea
The Mi'raj is a miracle, not a natural event.

The Mi'raj (Night Journey) is a fundamental belief in Sunni Islam, but the Mu'tazila raised a rational objection: how could a physical body ascend to the heavens? In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents their argument and the Sunni reply, grounding the response in the Qur'an and the principle of divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of Qur'anic verses.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite argument
The passage contains two parts: a scriptural argument from Qur'an 17:60 and a rational argument based on human nature. Find both.
The Mu'tazila interpret 'ru'ya' as 'dream'.
3
Identify the Sunni response
Nasafi gives two points: first, distinguishing dream from wakefulness; second, that the Prophet was taken up by God's power, not by his own nature.
The response shifts from natural possibility to divine omnipotence.
4
Reflect on the role of divine power
Consider why the Sunni response emphasizes that the Prophet was 'made to ascend' (ʿurija bihi). How does this answer the Mu'tazilite objection?
For Sunni theology, God's power can override natural laws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة احتجوا لنفي المعراج بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلْنَا الرُّيْ يَا الَّتِي أَرَيْنَكَ إِلَّا فِتْنَةً لِلنَّاسِ ﴾ [الإسراء: ٦٠] وقالوا: المعراج كان في الرُّؤْيا؛ لأن العقل لا يقبل ذلك، والعقل حُجَةُ الله على خلقه، لأن الله تعالى خلق آدم على صورة كثيفة، ومن طبعه الشقول والهبوط، وأما العُلو فمن طبع الطيور، فلهذا لا يصح المعراج. والجواب عنه أن نقول: الكافر يرى نفسه في المنام أنه في السماء، وإنما يظهر تخصيص النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إن كان له ذلك في اليقظة. وأما قوله من طبعه الشقول». قلت: نعم، ولكن هو لا يصعد بنفسه وإنما عُرِجَ به، لقوله تعالى: ﴿سُبْحَانَ الَّذِي
And the Mu'tazila argued for the denial of the Mi'raj by His statement Most High: 'And We did not make the vision which We showed you except as a trial for the people' [al-Isra:60]. And they said: The Mi'raj was in the dream; because reason cannot accept that, and reason is God's proof upon His creation. Because God Most High created Adam in a dense form, and from his nature is [؟] and descent, while ascent is from the nature of birds. For this reason the Mi'raj is not correct. The answer to that is that we say: The disbeliever sees himself in a dream that he is in heaven. Rather, the special distinction of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is shown if that happened to him in wakefulness. As for his statement 'from his nature is [؟]' – I say: Yes, but he does not ascend by himself; rather, he was made to ascend (ʿurija bihi), according to His statement Most High: 'Exalted is He who' [al-Isra:1].
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite objection against the physical Mi'raj and the Sunni response. The Mu'tazila cite Qur'an 17:60: 'And We did not make the vision (ru'ya) which We showed you except as a trial for the people.' They interpret 'ru'ya' as 'dream' and conclude that the entire Mi'raj was a dream, not a physical event. Their rational argument is that a human body, being dense (kathif) and naturally inclined to descent (hobut), cannot ascend to the heavens; only birds have the natural capacity for ascent. Therefore, a physical ascension is impossible. The Sunni response, introduced by 'والجواب عنه أن نقول', makes two points: (1) Even disbelievers dream of being in the sky, so dreaming it is not special. The Prophet's distinction is precisely that it occurred in wakefulness, not in a dream. (2) Regarding the claim about human nature (the text says 'الشقول' likely a corruption of 'الثقل' = heaviness), the Sunni concedes the human body is heavy, but the Prophet did not ascend by his own power; rather, he was taken up (ʿurija bihi) by divine power. The proof-text is the beginning of Surah al-Isra ('سبحان الذي أسرى بعبده'), which emphasizes that God is the agent of the journey. The argument thus shifts from natural possibility to divine omnipotence.

Key Terms
الرؤيا
العقل
حجة الله
صورة كثيفة
الهبوط
عُرج به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila claim that the Mi'raj was a dream because the verse uses 'ru'ya' which they interpret as dream.
Correction: The Sunnis hold that the Prophet's vision occurred in wakefulness, as indicated by the response that distinguishes his case from ordinary dreams.

Checkpoint
What is the core Mu'tazilite rational objection against a physical Mi'raj?
A) There is no hadith about it.
B) The human body is dense and naturally descends, so ascent is impossible.
C) The Prophet was not worthy of such an honor.
D) It contradicts the Qur'anic description of God's throne.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argued the Mi'raj was a dream; the Sunnis affirm it as a physical miracle accomplished by divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazila argument that the Mi'raj is impossible because it violates natural laws, do you ever secretly lean toward their reasoning? What does that reveal about your trust in God's absolute power over nature?
?
The Prophet did not ascend by himself; he was 'made to ascend.' What area of your life are you trying to 'ascend' by your own efforts instead of surrendering to being carried by divine power?
?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila Denial of the Physical Mi'raj
Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila argument that the Mi'raj was only a dream using Quranic verse and analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objections to the physical Mi'raj and Imam Nasafi's refutation using Quranic evidence and rational analogies.

Core Idea
Physical ascension is possible through divine power, overcoming natural heaviness.

Imam Nasafi addresses a key objection from the Mu'tazila who argued that the Mi'raj was only a dream because human nature is heavy and cannot ascend. This passage presents their reasoning and then provides a powerful refutation through Quranic verses and analogies, demonstrating that divine agency can override natural properties.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
Read the Mu'tazila objection: they cite the Quranic verse 'ru'ya' and argue that human heaviness prevents physical ascent.
Notice they base their argument on a Quranic verse and rational principle.
2
Examine the first refutation regarding 'ru'ya'
Imam Nasafi responds that 'ru'ya' can mean vision, not necessarily dream, and even if dream, the Prophet's distinction is in wakefulness.
3
Examine the second refutation regarding heaviness
Imam Nasafi explains that the Prophet did not ascend by himself; he was carried by Buraq and guided by Allah, using analogies of a thrown stone and an arrow.
These analogies show external force overcomes natural tendency.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the physical Mi'raj is rationally possible through divine power, and the Mu'tazila argument is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة احتجوا لنفي المعراج بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلْنَا الرُّؤْيَا الَّتِي أَرَيْنَاكَ إِلَّا فِتْنَةً لِلنَّاسِ﴾ [الإسراء: 60] وقالوا: المعراج كان في الرؤيا؛ لأن العقل لا يقبل ذلك، والعقل حجة الله على خلقه، لأن الله تعالى خلق آدم على صورة كثيفة، ومن طبعه الشغول [؟] والهبوط، وأما العلو فمن طبع الطيور، فلهذا لا يصح المعراج. والجواب عنه أن نقول: الكافر يرى نفسه في المنام أنه في السماء، وإنما يظهر تخصيص النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إن كان له ذلك في اليقظة. وأما قوله من طبعه الشغول [؟]. قلت: نعم، ولكن هو لا يصعد بنفسه وإنما عرج به، لقوله تعالى: ﴿سُبْحَانَ الَّذِي أَسْرَى بِعَبْدِهِ لَيْلًا﴾ [الإسراء: 1] ولم يقل إنه سرى بنفسه، ألا ترى أن الحجر والمدر من طبعه الشغول [؟]، ومع هذا إذا رماه إنسان يصعد الهواء، فالنبي عليه السلام إذا كان مركبه البراق وجبريل سائقه، والله تعالى هاديه، أولا يصعد السماء؟! وكذلك من اتخذ قوسا يمكن له أن يرمي به السهم في الهواء، فالنبي عليه السلام إذا كان الشرى [؟] قوسه، ومركبه البراق، وجاز به جبريل بإذن الله أولا يجاوز؟
The Mu'tazila argued for the denial of the Mi'raj with His statement: 'And We did not make the vision which We showed you except as a trial for the people' (17:60). They said: The Mi'raj was in a dream, because reason does not accept that, and reason is Allah's proof upon His creation. Because Allah created Adam in a dense form, and his nature is heaviness [؟] and descent, while elevation is the nature of birds, therefore the Mi'raj is not valid. The answer to that is: We say: The disbeliever sees himself in a dream that he is in the sky, and it only appears that the Prophet is distinguished if that happens to him while awake. As for his saying 'his nature is heaviness [؟]': I say: Yes, but he does not ascend by himself, rather he was taken up (ʿurija bihi), as per His statement: 'Exalted is He who took His servant by night' (17:1), and He did not say that he traveled by himself. Do you not see that stone and clay are of a heavy nature [؟], yet when a person throws them, they ascend through the air? So the Prophet (peace be upon him), if his mount was Al-Buraq and Gabriel was his driver, and Allah was his guide, would he not ascend to the sky?! Similarly, one who takes a bow can shoot an arrow into the air, so the Prophet if the night journey [؟] was his bow, his mount was Al-Buraq, and Gabriel conveyed him by Allah's permission, would he not pass through?
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The Mu'tazila object to the physical Mi'raj on two grounds: (1) The Quranic verse 'We did not make the vision... except as a trial' indicates it was only a dream. (2) Reason rejects that a human body, which is heavy, can ascend physically; ascending is only for birds. The author answers: (1) The word 'ru'ya' can mean a vision, not necessarily a dream; and even if it is a dream, that does not negate the physical Mi'raj—the Prophet was distinguished by having it also in wakefulness. (2) As for heaviness, the Prophet did not ascend by his own power; he was carried by Buraq and driven by Gabriel, with Allah as guide. Just as a thrown stone defies its nature to rise, so it is by Allah's power. The author uses analogies of a stone thrown upward and an arrow shot from a bow to show that external force can overcome natural heaviness. Thus the physical ascension is possible through divine agency.

Key Terms
المعتزلة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Physical ascent is impossible because humans are naturally heavy and cannot fly.
Correction: The Mi'raj was not accomplished by human power but by divine agency; external force (like throwing a stone or shooting an arrow) can overcome natural heaviness.

C) The Prophet never claimed it
D) It was only a story

Key Takeaway
The physical Mi'raj is not negated by human heaviness because divine agency can overcome natural properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila denied the physical Mi'raj because they thought it contradicted reason. Is there any teaching of Islam that you find hard to accept because it seems 'unreasonable' in your own mind? Will you submit to it anyway, or will you twist the text to fit your intellect? Make a concrete decision about one specific issue.
?
If you truly believed that Allah can lift the heaviest of things by His power, what burden in your life would you trust Him to lift? Write down one situation where you think ‘that’s impossible’ and then read the verse ‘Exalted is He who took His servant by night’—will you now ask Him to do the impossible for you?

Refutation of Mu'tazila Denial of Guardian Angels
Refutes the Mu'tazila claim that there are no guardian angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni argument for the existence of guardian angels as witnesses for the Day of Judgment, and why the Mu'tazila objection based on Allah's knowledge is insufficient.

Core Idea
Guardian angels are witnesses for us, not informants for Allah.

The Mu'tazila argued that since Allah already knows all deeds, there is no need for recording angels. The Sunni response reveals a deeper wisdom: the angels are not for Allah's knowledge but for our accountability. This passage from Imam Nasafi refutes that misconception and establishes the purpose of the 'guardian angels' (حفظة) as a proof against the servant on the Day of Resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Read the passage where the Mu'tazila state their position: since Allah knows everything and does not need information, there is no need for guardian angels. Notice their logic.
They assume the only purpose would be to inform Allah.
2
Examine the Sunni response
Read the response starting with 'قلنا'. The answer is that the angels are appointed as witnesses for the Day of Judgment, not for informing Allah.
3
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider why the existence of recorded deeds and witnesses is important for justice on the Day of Resurrection, even though Allah already knows.
This shows that reward and punishment are based on evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة: ليس علينا ملائكةٌ ولا حَفَظَة، وكلُّ ما يعمل الإنسان فالله تعالى عالم به يغفر لمن يشاء، ويعذب من يشاء، وإنما يحتاج إلى الحفظة أن لو كان جاهلاً، ولا يعلم ماذا يعمل عباده، والله تعالى لا يحتاج إلى أن يوكل عليهم ليكتب أعمالهم. قلنا: إنما يوكل عليهم ليكون حجةً على العبد يوم القيامة، فإذا أنكر العبد الأفعال يشهد عليه الملكان، وإذا نسي يكون الكتاب حجة عليه.
The Mu'tazila said: There are no angels and no guardians upon us. Everything that a person does—Allah Most High knows it; He forgives whom He wills, and punishes whom He wills. There would be a need for guardians only if He were ignorant and did not know what His servants do, and Allah Most High does not need to appoint them to write down their deeds. We said: He only appoints them so that it might be a proof against the servant on the Day of Resurrection. For if the servant denies the deeds, the two angels will testify against him, and if he forgets, the book will be a proof against him.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila and the Sunni response. The Mu'tazila argue that since Allah already knows all deeds and does not need information, there is no purpose in having guardian angels to record deeds. The Sunni answer is that the angels are not appointed for Allah's own knowledge (since He knows all), but to serve as witnesses against the servant on the Day of Judgment. If a person denies or forgets his deeds, the angels and the written record will stand as proof. Thus, the benefit is for the servant, not for Allah.

المعتزلة
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Guardian angels exist to inform Allah of our deeds, otherwise they are unnecessary.
Correction: The passage clarifies that angels are witnesses for the servant on the Day of Resurrection, not informants for Allah.

Checkpoint

A) To inform Allah of what the servant does, since Allah does not know everything.
B) To protect the servant from physical harm.
C) To serve as a witness against the servant on the Day of Resurrection.
D) To punish the servant immediately for sins.

Key Takeaway
Guardian angels are appointed to serve as witnesses for the Day of Judgment, ensuring that every deed is accounted for with proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The angels are witnesses for your accountability. Have you ever considered that your forgetfulness does not erase your deeds? How will you change your approach to repentance knowing that your sins remain recorded until you sincerely turn back?

Refutation of the Annihilation of Paradise and Hell
Nasafi refutes the Jahmiyya claim that Paradise and Hell will be annihilated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni argument against the Jahmiyya claim that Paradise and Hell will be annihilated, and the reasoning that annihilation would imply injustice on God's part.

Core Idea
If God is perfectly just, the eternal abodes must be permanent.

The Jahmiyya sect held a controversial view that Paradise and Hell would eventually be annihilated after fulfilling their purpose. They cited a Quranic verse and a prophetic hadith as evidence. In this passage, Imam Nasafi refutes their claim by reaffirming the eternity of both abodes and adding a key argument from divine justice: the impossibility of God committing injustice or tyranny.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Jahmiyya claim
Read the passage to grasp what the Jahmiyya believed about the fate of Paradise and Hell after the inhabitants have received their due.
Note that they used both a verse (57:3) and a hadith as proof.
2
Analyze the Sunni response
Identify the two-fold Sunni counter-argument: the eternity of the abodes and the impossibility of injustice from God.
The passage states that reward and punishment abodes cannot perish, and that annihilation would be unjust.
3
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this argument upholds God's perfect justice and the seriousness of ultimate reward and punishment.
This is a key point in Sunni eschatology (samiyyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الرد على القائلين بفناء الجنة والنار قالت الجهمية : إذا دخل أهل الجنةِ الجنةَ، وأهل النار النار، استمتعوا أهل الجنة بقدر أعمالهم، وأهل النار أذاقهم الله العذاب بقدر أعمالهم وكفرهم، ثم إن الله تعالى يُفني الجنة والنار. واحتجوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ هُوَ الْأَوَّلُ وَالْآخِرُ وَالظَّهِرُ وَالْبَاطِنُ ﴾ [الحديد: ٣] على ما ذكرنا، وعن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «سيأتي على جهنم يوم تصفّق الريح أبوابها وليس فيها أحد». وقال أهل السنة والجماعة: الجنة والنار دارا الخلد، وهما للثواب والعقاب فلا يفنيان على ما ذكرنا، ولأنه لا يجوز منه الظلم والجور
Section on the refutation of those who say that Paradise and Hell perish. The Jahmiyya said: When the inhabitants of Paradise enter Paradise and the inhabitants of Hell enter Hell, the inhabitants of Paradise enjoy according to their deeds, and the inhabitants of Hell – Allah makes them taste punishment according to their deeds and disbelief; then Allah annihilates Paradise and Hell. And they argued with His statement: 'He is the First and the Last, the Manifest and the Hidden' (Quran 57:3) as we mentioned, and from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'There will come a time upon Hell when the wind slams its doors and there is no one in it.' And Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a said: Paradise and Hell are the two abodes of eternity, and they are for reward and punishment, so they do not perish, as we mentioned, and because injustice and tyranny are not permissible from Him.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific Jahmiyya claim. The Jahmiyya argue that after people have entered Paradise and Hell, they will receive their due reward or punishment, but then Allah will annihilate both abodes entirely. Their evidence is twofold: first, the same Quranic verse used by the Mu'tazila (Quran 57:3) to argue for God's exclusive permanence; second, a prophetic hadith stating that Hell will one day have its doors banging empty. The response of Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a reasserts that Paradise and Hell are eternal abodes for reward and punishment, so they cannot be annihilated. They add a new argument: annihilation would imply that God deals unjustly or tyrannically (ظلم وجور) with the inhabitants, which is impossible for God. The underlying reasoning is that if reward and punishment are finite, then either God's justice is not complete or the deeds are not fully requited. By affirming eternal duration for both abodes, Ahl al-Sunna maintain that God's perfect justice demands permanent consequences for ultimate faith or disbelief.

Key Terms
الجهمية
فناء الجنة
فناء النار
الظلم
الجور
الخلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the additional Sunni argument against the Jahmiyya view presented in this passage?
A) The hadith is weak
B) Annihilation would imply injustice on God's part
C) The souls are eternal
D) The Throne prevents annihilation

Key Takeaway
Paradise and Hell are eternal because God's justice requires that ultimate reward and punishment be permanent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Paradise and Hell are eternal, how would your priorities change starting today? Is there any sin you excuse because you think the consequences are temporary?
?
The Jahmiyya thought they could limit God's punishment to finite time. When you imagine standing before Allah, do you bargain with His justice, expecting leniency without true repentance? What would it mean to take the eternity of Hell as seriously as the eternity of Paradise?
?
You have been taught that Paradise and Hell are permanent. How does this truth confront your casual attitude toward obedience and sin? Are you living as if the abodes are real and permanent, or as if they are distant and uncertain?

The Jahmiyya Argument for the Annihilation of Paradise and Hell
Nasafi presents the Jahmiyya argument and their two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jahmiyya claim that Paradise and Hell will be annihilated and the two proofs they use to support this claim.

Core Idea
The Jahmiyya argue that only Allah is eternal, so Paradise and Hell must eventually perish.

The Jahmiyya sect proposed a controversial view that even Paradise and Hell, despite being created for eternal reward and punishment, will eventually be annihilated after their inhabitants have received their due. In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents their argument and the two proofs they cite from the Quran and hadith. Understanding their reasoning is essential before examining the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Jahmiyya argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the claim, then the two proofs.
The Jahmiyya claim is that after reward and punishment, Allah will annihilate Paradise and Hell.
2
Examine the first proof
The Jahmiyya cite Quran 57:3 ('He is the First and the Last...') to argue that only Allah remains forever. Understand how they interpret this verse to imply the annihilation of all created things, including Paradise and Hell.
3
Examine the second proof
They also cite a hadith about a day when Hell's doors will blow empty. Identify how they use this to support the idea that Hell will be vacated and then annihilated.
Note the common misunderstanding: the hadith likely refers to a temporary emptying, not total annihilation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الرد على القائلين بفناء الجنة والنار
قالت الجهمية : إذا دخل أهل الجنةِ الجنةَ، وأهل النار النار، استمتعوا أهل الجنة بقدر أعمالهم، وأهل النار أذاقهم الله العذاب بقدر أعمالهم وكفرهم، ثم إن الله تعالى يُفني الجنة والنار.
واحتجوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ هُوَ الْأَوَّلُ وَالْآخِرُ وَالظَّهِرُ وَالْبَاطِنُ ﴾ [الحديد: ٣] على ما ذكرنا، وعن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «سيأتي على جهنم يوم تصفّق الريح أبوابها وليس فيها أحد».
Chapter on refuting those who claim the annihilation of Paradise and Hell. The Jahmiyya said: When the people of Paradise enter Paradise, and the people of Hell enter Hell, the people of Paradise will enjoy [it] according to their deeds, and the people of Hell will taste punishment according to their deeds and disbelief. Then Allah will annihilate Paradise and Hell. And they adduced the saying of Allah Most High: 'He is the First and the Last, the Manifest and the Hidden' (Al-Hadid 3) as we have mentioned, and from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'There will come upon Hell a day when the wind blows its doors and there is no one in it.'
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the Jahmiyya sect regarding the eventual annihilation of Paradise and Hell. The Jahmiyya claim that after the inhabitants of Paradise and Hell have received their due reward or punishment according to their deeds, Allah will bring an end to both realms. They support their claim with two proofs: first, the Quranic verse 'He is the First and the Last, the Manifest and the Hidden,' implying that only Allah remains eternal and everything else will perish. This verse, they argue, indicates the annihilation of all created things, including Paradise and Hell. Second, they cite a prophetic hadith that foretells a time when Hell will be empty, with its doors flapping in the wind, suggesting that Hell itself will eventually be vacant and thus annihilated. The author sets up this objection in order to refute it in the subsequent lesson.

فناء الجنة والنار
هو الأول والآخر
حديث مجيء يوم على جهنم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jahmiyya believe that the hadith about Hell's doors blowing empty proves total annihilation of Hell.
Correction: The hadith likely refers to a temporary emptying of Hell after punishment ends, not its absolute annihilation.

Checkpoint

C) The verse 'Allah does not wrong anyone' and a hadith about paradise
D) The verse 'And the earth will shine with the light of its Lord' and a hadith about the bridge

Key Takeaway
The Jahmiyya base their claim of annihilation on a Quranic verse and a hadith, but these proofs are later refuted.

The Origin of the Mu'tazila and Their Five Principles
Nasafi relates the founders of Mu'tazila and lists their five principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical origin of the Mu'tazila and the core principles that define their doctrine.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila began with Wasil ibn 'Ata and were defined by five principles.

This passage from Nasafi's Bahr al-Kalam traces the roots of the Mu'tazilite school. It identifies the early figures and highlights their system of belief, centered on a book called 'The Five Principles.' As we read, note that only four principles are explicitly listed—the fifth is implied. This lesson will help you grasp the historical context and the key tenets of Mu'tazilism.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the story of the Mu'tazila's origin.
Pay attention to the names and the flow of events.
2
Identify the key figures
Note the names Wasil ibn 'Ata, 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd, and Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf. See how each contributed to the development of the school.
Wasil is the first, 'Amr is his follower, Abu Hudhayl is the systematizer.
3
Examine the five principles
Even though only four are listed, understand that the fifth principle is well-known. Reflect on why the author might have omitted it.
The missing fifth principle is 'al-amr bi-l-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
في ذكر أول من قال بالاعتزال
قال الشيخ الإمام الأجَلُّ رحمه الله : أول من تكلم في مذهب الاعتزال رجل يقال له واصل بن عطاء، وتابعه عمرو بن عبيد تلميذ الحسن البصري. فلما كان في زمن هارون الرشيد خرج أبو هذيل العلاف، وصنف لهم كتابا وبين مذهبهم وجمع علومهم، وسمى ذلك الكتاب الأصول الخمسة، وكلما رأوا رجلا قالوا له خفية: هل قرأت الأصول الخمسة؟ فإن قال: نعم، عرفوا أنه على مذهبهم. والأصول الخمسة: العدل، والتوحيد، والوعد والوعيد، ومسألة البين البين.
Section on the mention of the first who spoke of Mu'tazilism.
The most great imam, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The first who spoke in the Mu'tazilite doctrine was a man called Wasil ibn 'Ata, and he was followed by 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd, the student of al-Hasan al-Basri. Then, in the time of Harun al-Rashid, Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf emerged, and he compiled a book for them, explained their doctrine, collected their sciences, and named that book 'The Five Principles'. Whenever they saw a man, they would say to him secretly: Have you read the Five Principles? If he said yes, they knew he was of their doctrine. And the Five Principles are: Justice, Unity, Promise and Threat, and the matter of the intermediate state (al-bayn bayn).
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative. Nasafi identifies Wasil ibn 'Ata as the first to articulate the Mu'tazilite position, followed by 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd. Later, under Harun al-Rashid, Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf systematized their teachings into a book titled 'The Five Principles'. The Mu'tazila used this book as a test: if a person had read it, they were considered adherents. The principles listed are: justice (al-'adl), unity (al-tawhid), promise and threat (al-wa'd wa al-wa'id), and the matter of the intermediate state (mas'alat al-bayn bayn). The text only mentions four, but the title says five; the fifth principle (al-amr bi-l-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar) is omitted here, possibly because it was well-known or the author considered it implied.

Key Terms
الاعتزال
الأصول الخمسة
العدل
التوحيد
الوعد والوعيد
مسألة البين بين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila only have four principles.
Correction: They actually have five; the author only lists four here due to assumed knowledge. The fifth is 'al-amr bi-l-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar'.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila originated with Wasil ibn 'Ata and were systematized by Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf into a creed centered on five principles, though only four are listed in this passage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Mu'tazila defined themselves by five clear principles. Do you have equally clear principles that define your faith? In what areas of your life do you avoid defining your beliefs precisely, perhaps to escape accountability?
?
The passage shows a gradual development of the Mu'tazila creed. Reflect: How did your own understanding of Islamic creed develop? Was it through critical study or blind acceptance? Are you willing to revisit and examine the foundations of your faith as these scholars did?

The Four Mu'tazilite Principles on Justice, Quran, Promise, and Threat
Nasafi enumerates the four Mu'tazilite principles of al-adl, createdness of the Quran, wa'd, and wa'id.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four foundational principles of the Mu'tazila regarding divine justice, the createdness of the Quran, and the necessity of fulfilling promises and threats.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila built their theology on four rational principles that define God's justice and speech.

Nasafi introduces the core doctrines of the Mu'tazila, a major theological school in Islam. These four principles—justice, createdness of the Quran, promise, and threat—form the backbone of their rationalist approach. Understanding them is key to grasping later Sunni critiques and defenses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice how each principle is introduced with 'أما'.
2
Identify the four principles
List the four principles: al-adl, the Quran as created, wa'd (promise), wa'id (threat). Note the reasoning given for each.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each
For each principle, identify the rational argument the Mu'tazila present. For example, for justice, they argue that if God created evil and then punished for it, that would be unjust.
This shows their reliance on reason to define God's attributes.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how these principles shape the Mu'tazilite view of God's attributes and actions. Ask yourself: why would they insist that the Quran is created?
This connects to the broader debate on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العدل: قالوا بأن الله تعالى لا يخلق الشر، ولا يقضى به؛ لأنه لو خلق الشر وقضى به ثم يعذبهم على ذلك يكون ذلك منه جورًا، والله تعالى عادل لا يجور. وأما الثاني: قالوا بأن القرآن مخلوق وكذا سائر صفاته؛ لأنا لو قلنا بأنه غير مخلوق لا يكون توحيدا. وأما الثالث: قالوا بأن الله تعالى إذا وعد عباده ثوابًا لا يجوز أن يخالف وعده؛ لأن الله تعالى لا يخلف الميعاد. والرابع: وإذا أوعد وعيدًا لا يجوز أن لا يعذبهم ويخالف وعيده؛ لأن الخلف في كلام الله تعالى لا يجوز.
As for justice: they said that Allah, exalted is He, does not create evil, nor does He decree it; because if He created evil and decreed it, then punished them for it, that would be injustice from Him, and Allah is just, He does not commit injustice. As for the second: they said that the Quran is created, and likewise all of His attributes; because if we said that it is not created, that would not be tawhid (monotheism). As for the third: they said that if Allah, exalted is He, promises His servants a reward, it is not permissible for Him to break His promise; because Allah does not break the appointment. And the fourth: if He threatens a threat, it is not permissible for Him not to punish them and break His threat; because breaking (the promise) in the speech of Allah is not permissible.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage lists four Mu'tazilite theological principles. The first principle is 'al-adl' (justice): the Mu'tazila assert that Allah does not create evil or decree it, because doing so would make Him unjust. The second principle is the createdness of the Quran and all divine attributes, for to say they are uncreated would compromise tawhid (monotheism) by implying eternal entities besides Allah. The third principle concerns promise (wa'd): if Allah promises a reward to believers, He must fulfill it; He cannot break a promise. The fourth principle concerns threat (wa'id): similarly, if Allah threatens punishment for a sin, He must carry it out; breaking a threat is also impermissible for Him. Each principle is based on a rational argument about what befits God's justice and perfection.

Key Terms
العدل
القرآن مخلوق
الوعد
الوعيد
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite view on divine justice implies a limitation on God's power over evil.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that God's justice prevents Him from creating evil, but they do not see this as a limitation; rather, it is a necessary consequence of His perfect justice.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite principle on promise (wa'd), what is Allah obligated to do if He promises a reward?
A) He may break the promise out of mercy.
B) He must fulfill the promise.
C) He may delay the reward.
D) He may replace it with something else.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila base their theology on four rational principles: divine justice excludes creating evil, the Quran is created, and God must fulfill both promises and threats.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see or experience evil in the world, do you instinctively attribute it to God's will or to human choices? How does the Mu'tazilite argument that God's justice forbids Him from creating evil challenge your default response? What would change in your outlook if you adopted their view?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that the Quran must be created to preserve God's oneness. Have you ever felt that affirming the eternity of God's attributes, like His speech, strains your understanding of tawhid? How do you reconcile the eternity of the Quran with the absolute oneness of God?
?
The Mu'tazila teach that God cannot break His word, whether promise or threat. Are you living today with the same certainty that God's threats (like punishment for sin) are as binding as His promises? Where in your life are you taking God's mercy for granted, assuming He will not hold you to His words?

Sunni Response on Divine Threats
Nasafi presents the Sunni position that Allah may forgive those threatened, rebutting the Mu'tazilite claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrinal stance on divine threats and how it refutes the Mu'tazilite objection through the distinction between a promise and a threat.

Core Idea
Allah may forgive His threats; a promise obligates, but a threat is a warning subject to mercy.

This passage addresses a critical point of contention between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila: does Allah's threat of punishment bind Him to punish? The Sunnis argue that a threat, unlike a promise, does not create a right that demands fulfillment; Allah may pardon as an act of grace. The Mu'tazilite proof texts are explained away by a broader Quranic principle of forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the complete Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the overall argument without overanalyzing.
Notice the structure: first the Sunni claim, then the Mu'tazilite objection, then the answer.
2
Identify the Sunni claim and Mu'tazilite objection
Locate where Nasafi states the Sunni position that Allah may pardon even after a threat, and the two Quranic verses the Mu'tazila use to argue that punishment is inevitable.
The Mu'tazila cite 4:93 and 4:30 as evidence.
3
Examine the first part of the answer
The answer begins by noting that the threat verses are qualified by 4:48 (and 4:116), which explicitly says Allah forgives anything except shirk for whom He wills. This shows the threat is not absolute.
This is a classic Sunni counter: broader Quranic context limits specific threats.
4
Examine the second part: promise vs. threat
The passage then distinguishes between a promise (wa'd) and a threat (wa'id). A promise creates a right for the servant, so breaking it would be blameworthy. A threat does not create such a right; pardoning is an act of grace, not a breach.
This distinction is central to Sunni theology of divine justice.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: Sunni theology emphasizes both divine mercy and justice without contradiction. The Mu'tazilite position is seen as overly rigid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أهل السنة والجماعة: إن الله تعالى إذا أوعد وعيدا يجوز أن لا يعذبهم، ولكنه يعفو ويغفر لهم ولا يعاقبهم. واحتجت المعتزلة بقوله تعالى: {وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَالِدًا فِيهَا وَغَضِبَ اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَلَعَنَهُ وَأَعَدَّ لَهُ عَذَابًا عَظِيمًا} وكذلك قوله تعالى: {وَمَن يَفْعَلْ ذَلِكَ عُدْوَانًا وَظُلْمًا فَسَوْفَ نُصْلِيهِ نَارًا}. والجواب عنه قلنا ما ذكر الله تعالى من الوعيد صار مستثنى بقوله تعالى: {إِنَّ اللَّهَ لا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ}. وقولهم: يكون خلفًا في الوعيد. قلنا: لا يكون ذلك خلفاً في الوعيد، بل يعفو عنه كرماً وفضلاً، بخلاف ما إذا وعد الثواب حيث لا يجوز أن يخلف وعده؛ لأن ذلك حق العبد؛ فلو جاز ذلك يكون لوماً ولا يُعد كرمًا، وهذا لا يُظن بالله عز وجل.
And the Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: Indeed Allah, exalted is He, if He threatens a threat, it is permissible for Him not to punish them, but He pardons and forgives them and does not punish them. The Mu'tazila argued with the saying of the Exalted: {And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein, and Allah's wrath is upon him, and He has cursed him and prepared for him a great punishment} and also His saying: {And whoever does that in aggression and injustice, soon We will roast him in a Fire}. The answer to it: we said that what Allah mentioned of the threat is made an exception by His saying: {Indeed Allah does not forgive associating partners with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills}. And their saying: 'That would be a breach of the threat.' We said: That is not a breach of the threat, rather He pardons him as generosity and favor, contrary to when He promises a reward, where it is not permissible for Him to break His promise, because that is the right of the servant; if that were permissible, it would be blameworthy and not counted as generosity, and this is not thought of Allah, mighty and majestic.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni position on divine threats and responds to the Mu'tazilite objection. First, the Ahl al-Sunna state that when Allah threatens punishment, He may choose not to punish, but instead pardon and forgive. The Mu'tazila object by citing two Quranic verses (4:93 and 4:30) that threaten eternal punishment for certain sins, arguing that Allah must fulfill His threats. The Sunni answer has two parts. First, the threat in these verses is qualified by the verse 4:48 (and 4:116) which explicitly states that Allah forgives anything except shirk for whomever He wills. Thus the threat is not absolute. Second, in response to the claim that such non-fulfillment would be a breach (khulf) of the threat, the Sunnis argue that a threat is not like a promise. A promise creates a right for the servant, so breaking it would be blameworthy. A threat, however, is a warning; if Allah forgives, it is an act of grace (karm wa fadl), not a breach. Therefore, it is not possible to attribute such blame to Allah.

Key Terms
أهل السنة والجماعة
الوعيد
العفو
الخلف
الوعد
حق العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni view allows God to break His word.
Correction: Sunnis distinguish between a promise (which obligates fulfillment) and a threat (which is a warning subject to mercy). Pardoning is not a breach but an act of grace.

Checkpoint
What is the Sunni justification for why Allah may not carry out a threat of punishment?
A) The threat was never serious.
B) The threat is overridden by the verse on forgiveness for anything except shirk.
C) The servant can demand mercy as a right.
D) Allah is not bound by His own words.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Sunna affirm that Allah's threats are not absolute; He may forgive those who deserve punishment, as an act of grace, while promises of reward are binding as a right of the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that Allah's mercy can override His threat? Or do you live in a state of despair, as if the threat is absolute? How does this lesson shape your hope in Allah's forgiveness?
?
This lesson shows that Allah's promise is binding because it becomes the servant's right. Do you live as though you have a claim on Allah's reward? Or do you rely solely on His grace, knowing that even your best deeds are insufficient?
?
The threat of punishment applies to major sins like murder, yet Allah may forgive. Is there any sin in your life that you think is beyond Allah's forgiveness? How does this lesson challenge that assumption about shirk?

Refuting the Intermediate State: The Case of Walid ibn Uqbah
Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili claim of an intermediate state for grave sinners by interpreting a verse about the believer and the wicked.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili doctrine of the intermediate state and how Imam Nasafi refutes it by citing the specific occasion of revelation for a key verse.

Core Idea
The verse that seems to support an intermediate state actually describes a hypocrite, not a middle category.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a key Mu'tazili belief: that a person who commits a major sin falls into an intermediate state between faith and disbelief. The Mu'tazila use a Quranic verse to support this claim, but Nasafi explains the specific context of that verse, showing it refers to a hypocrite, not a general intermediate category. This refutation is based on the occasion of revelation (sabab al-nuzul).

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili Claim
The Mu'tazila assert that a committer of a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, but in an intermediate state. They base this on a Quranic verse that distinguishes between believer and wicked.
This position is called 'al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn.'
2
Read the Arabic Passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Note the Mu'tazili argument and then the refutation. Pay attention to the key terms like 'al-bayn bayn' and 'al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn'.
The refutation begins with 'Wal-jawab...'
3
Analyze the Refutation
Imam Nasafi responds by explaining the verse was revealed about Walid ibn Uqbah, a hypocrite. Ali (RA) called him a disbeliever, and Allah revealed the verse agreeing with Ali. Thus, the verse does not establish an intermediate state.
The verse distinguishes between a true believer and a hypocrite (who is actually a disbeliever), not between two general categories.
4
Reflect on the Implications
Consider why this refutation is important: it defends the orthodox view that major sins do not remove one from faith entirely (contrary to the Mu'tazila) nor make one a disbeliever (contrary to the Khawarij).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مسألة البين بين قالوا بأن مرتكب الكبيرة تخرج من الإيمان ولا يدخل في الكفر عندهم، بل يكون له منزلةٌ بين المنزلتين، واحتجوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤْمِناً كَمَن كَانَ فَاسِقَا لَا يَسْتَوُونَ ﴾ [السجدة: ۱۸] فَصَلَ بين المؤمن والفاسق، فثَبَتَ أنه ليس من هذا ولا من ذلك. والجواب عن قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤْمِناً كَمَن كَانَ فَاسِقَا لَّا يَسْتَوُونَ أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا كَمَن كَانَ فَاسِقَا لَّا يَستَونَ ﴾ [السجدة: ۱۸] هذه الآية نزلت في حق وليد بن عقبة المنافق لعنه الله حين قال لعلي رضي الله عنه: إن كان لك لسان وقوة ومنظر فلي أيضًا لسان وقوة ومنظر، فقال له علي رضي الله عنه: اسكت فإنك كافر» فأنزل الله تعالى هذه الآية موافقًا لقول علي رضي الله عنه.
As for the matter of al-bayn bayn (the intermediate), they said that the committer of a major sin exits from faith but does not enter into disbelief according to them; rather he has a station between the two stations. And they argued with His statement, the Exalted: 'Is he who was a believer like he who was a wicked? They are not equal' [As-Sajdah 32:18]. So he made a distinction between the believer and the wicked, so it is established that he is not from this nor from that. And the answer to His statement, the Exalted: 'Is he who was a believer like he who was a wicked? They are not equal' [As-Sajdah 32:18]: this verse was revealed concerning Walid ibn Uqbah the hypocrite, may Allah curse him, when he said to Ali, may Allah be pleased with him: 'If you have a tongue, strength, and appearance, I also have a tongue, strength, and appearance.' So Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, said to him: 'Be quiet, for you are a disbeliever.' Then Allah revealed this verse, agreeing with the statement of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage addresses the Mu'tazili doctrine of al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn (the intermediate state). The Mu'tazila claim that a person who commits a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, but in an intermediate position. They support this with the verse 'Is he who was a believer like he who was a wicked? They are not equal,' arguing that the verse distinguishes between believer and wicked, implying a third category. The response is that this verse was revealed about a specific individual, Walid ibn Uqbah, a hypocrite who challenged Ali. Ali called him a disbeliever, and Allah revealed the verse affirming that Ali was right. Therefore, the verse does not establish an intermediate state; rather it distinguishes between a true believer and a hypocrite (who is actually a disbeliever). The Mu'tazili interpretation is thus refuted by the specific occasion of revelation.

مرتكب الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Is he who was a believer like he who was a wicked?' proves there is an intermediate state between faith and disbelief.
Correction: The verse was revealed about a specific hypocrite, Walid ibn Uqbah, whom Ali (RA) called a disbeliever. It does not support a general intermediate category.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili doctrine of the intermediate state is refuted by showing that the verse they cite applies to a known hypocrite, not to a general category of major sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The verse separates the true believer from the wicked hypocrite. Are you living as if there is no middle ground—either you are with Allah or against Him? What is one area of your life where you have been comfortable in 'between'? Make a decision today to choose faith over convenience.
