Refuting Mu'tazilite Intercession Concepts
Nasafi presents Mu'tazilite divisions on shafa'a and refutes two categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes two Mu'tazilite categories of intercession by using their own principle of divine obligation.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite obligation removes the need for intercession.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi engages with the Mu'tazilite division on intercession (shafa'a). He first presents their threefold categorization of who benefits from intercession, then critiques the first two categories using the Mu'tazilites' own theological commitments. This approach demonstrates a powerful method of internal critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the three groups the Mu'tazilites affirm intercession for.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite categories
List the three categories: (1) those avoiding major sins but committing minor sins, (2) those committing major sins then repenting, (3) those avoiding all sins. Understand the need for intercession in each case.
3
Examine the refutation of the first category
Nasafi argues that on Mu'tazilite premises, avoiding major sins obligates God to forgive minor sins (Quran 4:31), so intercession is unnecessary. See how he uses their own proof text.
4
Examine the refutation of the second category
For the second, Nasafi argues that repentance from major sins obligates God to accept it, so intercession is again superfluous. Notice he uses the Mu'tazilite concept of wujub (obligation) against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات الشفاعة. تفرقت المعتزلة في الشَّفاعة، منهم من أنكر الشفاعة أصلاً ورأسا، ومنهم من أثبت الشفاعة لثلاث فِرَق منهم مَن اجتنب الكبائر، وارتكب الصغائر، فيحتاج إلى مغفرة الصغائر بشفاعة الأنبياء والملائكة صلوات الله عليهم. ومنهم من ارتكب الكبائر، ثم تاب عن ذلك، فيحتاج إلى قبول توبتهم بشفاعة الأنبياء والملائكة حتى يقبل الله تعالى توبتهم بشفاعتهم. ومنهم من اجتنب الكبائر والصغائر، فيحتاج إلى زيادة الدرجات على أعمالهم بشفاعة الأنبياء والملائكة، ولا شفاعة لغير هؤلاء. والجواب عن الفصل الأول: هذا على مذهبهم لا يصح؛ لأن عندهم أنَّ مَن اجتنب الكبائر فواجب على الله تعالى أن يغفر ذنوبهم البتة بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنكُمْ سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ وَنُدْخِلْكُم مُّدْخَلًا كَرِيمًا﴾ [النساء: 31] فلا يحتاج إلى الشفاعة. وأما الثاني: قالوا: إن من ارتكب الكبيرة ثم تاب فيحتاج إلى قبول توبته بشفاعة الأنبياء والملائكة. قلنا: هذا أيضاً على مذهبهم لا يصح، لأن عندهم كل من ارتكب كبيرة ثم تاب فواجب على الله تعالى قبول توبته لا محالة، فإذا وجب على الله تعالى قبول توبته فلا يحتاج إلى شفاعته.
Section on establishing intercession. The Mu'tazilites differed regarding intercession: some of them denied intercession entirely and completely, and some of them affirmed intercession for three groups: among them is one who avoids major sins and commits minor sins, so he needs forgiveness of minor sins through intercession of the prophets and angels, may God's blessings be upon them. And among them is one who commits major sins, then repents from that, so he needs acceptance of their repentance through intercession of the prophets and angels so that God Almighty accepts their repentance through their intercession. And among them is one who avoids major and minor sins, so he needs increase of degrees for their deeds through intercession of the prophets and angels, and there is no intercession for others besides these. The answer concerning the first category: this according to their school is not valid; because according to them, whoever avoids major sins, it is obligatory upon God Almighty to forgive their sins absolutely by His saying: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds and admit you to a noble entrance' [Quran 4:31], so he does not need intercession. As for the second: they said: whoever commits a major sin then repents needs acceptance of his repentance through intercession of the prophets and angels. We said: this also according to their school is not valid, because according to them everyone who commits a major sin then repents, it is obligatory upon God Almighty to accept his repentance without fail, so when it is obligatory upon God Almighty to accept his repentance, he does not need His intercession.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on intercession (shafa'a). The author first presents the Mu'tazilite division: they split into those who deny intercession altogether and those who affirm it for three specific categories of believers: (1) those who avoid major sins but commit minor sins – they need intercession for forgiveness of minor sins; (2) those who commit major sins and then repent – they need intercession for their repentance to be accepted; (3) those who avoid both major and minor sins – they need intercession for an increase in ranks. The author then refutes the first two categories using the Mu'tazilites' own principles. For the first category, the Mu'tazilites hold that avoiding major sins makes forgiveness of minor sins obligatory upon God, citing Quran 4:31. Therefore, such a person does not need intercession, as God must forgive him anyway. For the second category, the Mu'tazilites similarly hold that repentance from major sins makes acceptance of repentance obligatory upon God. Thus, after repentance, the person's repentance is already accepted by necessity, so intercession is superfluous. The refutation is effective because it turns the Mu'tazilite doctrine of divine obligation (wujub) against their own belief in intercession. The third category (those without sin) is left unrefuted here; the author moves on to the Sunni position in subsequent passages. The passage shows careful engagement with the opponent's framework.

Key Terms
الشفاعة
المعتزلة
الكبائر
الصغائر
التوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Nasafi rejects intercession altogether.
Correction: Nasafi only refutes the Mu'tazilite restricted version; he affirms intercession for believers (as will be shown later).

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the first Mu'tazilite group (those avoiding major sins) does not need intercession?
A) Because they are already forgiven.
B) Because according to the Mu'tazilites, God must forgive them without any intercession.
C) Because they do not believe in intercession.
D) Because they have committed minor sins only.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazilite views on intercession by showing that their doctrine of divine obligation makes intercession redundant for the categories they propose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you, like the Mu'tazilites, ever assume that your avoidance of major sins automatically guarantees forgiveness, forgetting your need for Allah's mercy and the intercession of His chosen ones? What would it mean for you to truly depend on Allah's grace rather than your own obedience?
?
How does the Mu'tazilite doctrine of obligation upon God strip away the very need for supplication and hope in His mercy? In your own prayers, are you making requests or demanding? Examine your heart and resolve to adopt a posture of humble request today.
?
This lesson shows that even repentance, if seen as an obligatory acceptance, removes the need for intercession. Do you treat your repentance as a transaction that compels Allah, or as a plea that requires His mercy? How will you change your attitude in your next sincere turning back to Allah?

Rationalist Objection to the Punishment of the Grave
The author presents the rationalist objection against punishment of the grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationalist objection against punishment of the grave as presented by the Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Jahmiyya, and the logical dichotomy they employ.

Core Idea
A rational dichotomy: flesh without soul cannot feel pain; soul re-entry requires a second death.

Before the Sunni response, the kalam tradition records a strong rationalist objection to the doctrine of punishment in the grave. Three sects—the Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Jahmiyya—argue that reason and analogy cannot accept this doctrine. Their argument is based on a simple but powerful dichotomy: either the body is punished without the soul (impossible) or the soul re-enters causing a second death (contradicting the Quran). This passage presents that objection in its classical form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: a dichotomy (either...or) and a Quranic proof.
The passage is a single argument with two rejected options.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The sects propose two ways Allah could punish in the grave: punish the flesh without the soul, or re-enter the soul and then punish. Identify why each is rejected.
The first is rejected because flesh without soul cannot feel pain; the second because it would require a second death.
3
Examine the Quranic citation
Find the verse from Surah Al Imran (3:185) and understand how it is used to rule out a second death. Why does this make the second possibility invalid?
The verse says every soul tastes death only once.
4
Understand the conclusion
With both options invalid, the only remaining conclusion is that there is no punishment in the grave. See how the argument forces this logical outcome.
This sets up the Sunni response that follows in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات عذاب القبر. قالت المعتزلة والنجارية والجهمية: عذاب القبر وسؤال منكر ونكير لا يقبله العقل والقياس، لأنه لو عذَّبه لا يخلو إما أن يُعذِّب اللحم بغير الروح، أو يدخل فيه الرُّوح ثم يعذبه، وباطل أن يعذَّب اللحم بغير الرُّوح؛ لأن اللحم بغير الرُّوح لا يتألم، وباطل أن تدخل فيه الرُّوح ثم يعذبه، لأنه لو دخل فيه الروح يحتاج إلى الموت. ثانياً: وهذا لا يجوز؛ لأن الله تعالى قال: كُل نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الموتِ ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٨٥] أخبر أنهم لا يذوقون فيها الموتَ إِلَّا مرةً واحدة، فإذا بطل القسمان تعيَّن القسم الثالث وهو أن لا يعذَّب في القبر.
Chapter on Proof of Punishment of the Grave. The Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Jahmiyya said: Punishment of the grave and the questioning of Munkar and Nakir are not accepted by reason and analogy, because if He punishes, it is either that He punishes the flesh without the soul, or He causes the soul to enter it and then punishes it. It is invalid that the flesh be punished without the soul, because flesh without the soul does not feel pain. And it is invalid that the soul enters it and then punishes it, because if the soul entered it, it would require a second death. And that is not permissible because Allah Most High said: 'Every soul will taste death' (Qur'an 3:185). He informed that they will not taste death in it except once. So when the two possibilities are invalidated, the third possibility is determined, which is that there is no punishment in the grave.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of three rationalist sects—the Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Jahmiyya—against the doctrine of punishment in the grave. They argue using a dichotomy: if Allah punishes in the grave, it must be either by punishing the flesh without the soul (which is impossible because flesh without the soul cannot feel pain) or by re-entering the soul into the body and then punishing it (which would require a second death, contradicting the Qur'anic verse that every soul tastes death only once). Since both options are invalid, they conclude that grave punishment cannot occur. This sets up the Sunni response that follows.

Key Terms
عذاب القبر
سؤال منكر ونكير
المعتزلة
النجارية
الجهمية
القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila argument is logically conclusive and leaves no room for a response.
Correction: The Sunni tradition affirms that after death, the soul has a continued connection to the body in the grave (barzakh), allowing for sensation and punishment without a second death, which resolves the dichotomy.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Jahmiyya, why is punishment of the grave rationally impossible?
A) Because the body decays and cannot feel pain.
B) Because it would require either punishing flesh without soul (impossible) or re-entering the soul causing a second death (contradicts Quran).
C) Because the Quran explicitly denies punishment in the grave.
D) Because the soul is not present in the grave at all.

Key Takeaway
The rationalist objection uses a dichotomy to argue that punishment of the grave is impossible, but this sets the stage for the Sunni theological response based on the barzakh (intermediate state).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazila argument, do you find it compelling? Does your heart waver at the thought that punishment after death might be irrational? What does that reveal about the strength of your reliance on revelation over reason?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected the punishment of the grave based on a logical dichotomy. Have you ever rejected a clear teaching of Islam because it seemed irrational to your limited mind? Will you now submit to Allah's words even when your reason struggles?
?
The verse 'Every soul will taste death' was used here to deny a second death. Are you living as if you have only one chance to prepare for what comes after? If the punishment of the grave is real, what are you doing today to avoid it?

The Inviolability of Muslim Blood
Classification of reasons for lawful killing of a Muslim by different sects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on when a Muslim's blood may be lawfully shed and how it differs from the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

Core Idea
Muslim life is inviolable except by clear textual evidence.

In this lesson, we examine a crucial issue of Islamic law: when is it permissible to shed the blood of a Muslim? The author presents the views of two deviant sects and then the position of Ahl al-Sunnah, grounded in a prophetic hadith. This comparison highlights the Sunni emphasis on preserving life and strict limitation of killing to specific causes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that this passage addresses a serious legal and theological issue: the sanctity of Muslim life and exceptions.
The term 'Ahl al-Qibla' refers to all Muslims who pray toward the Kaaba.
2
Read the Mu'tazila and Khawarij position
Note the four conditions under which they consider a Muslim's blood lawful: major sin, innovation, rebellion, and neglecting obligations.
They add a nuance: if one deems abandonment of an obligation permissible, consensus makes blood lawful.
3
Read the Ahl al-Sunnah position
Ahl al-Sunnah restrict lawful killing to three conditions from the hadith: apostasy after faith, adultery after marriage, and murder without right.
This shows the Sunni reliance on explicit textual evidence.
4
Note the ruling on rebels
Fighting a rebel (baghi) is allowed only while he fights; if he ceases, fighting must stop.
This is not a general permission to kill but a defensive measure.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider how this passage reinforces the sanctity of Muslim life and the importance of evidence in Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان حرمة دماء أهل القبلة قالت المعتزلة والخوارج : دماء أهل القبلة تحل بإحدى معانٍ أربعة: أحدها: إذا ارتكب الكبيرة. والثاني: إذا أحدث بدعة. والثالث: إذا سل سيفًا علي السلطان. والرابع: إذا عطّل فريضة أي إذا تركها، أما إذا استحل تركها فيحل دمه بالإجماع. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة : دماء أهل القبلة لا تحل إلا بإحدى معانٍ ثلاثة بالحديث وهو ما رُوي عن النبي أنه قال: «لا يحل دم امرئ مسلم إلا بإحدى ثلاث معان: كفر بعد إيمان، وزنا بعد إحصان، وقتل نفس بغير حق» . وأما إذا خرج باغيًا على السلطان يجوز قتاله ما دام يقاتله، فإذا ترك يُترك
Section on clarifying the inviolability of the blood of the people of the Qibla. The Mu'tazila and the Khawarij said: The blood of the people of the Qibla becomes lawful by one of four meanings: First: if he commits a major sin. Second: if he innovates a bid'ah. Third: if he draws a sword against the ruler. Fourth: if he neglects an obligation, i.e., abandons it; but if he deems its abandonment permissible, his blood becomes lawful by consensus. Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: The blood of the people of the Qibla is not lawful except by one of three meanings according to the hadith, which is what was narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'The blood of a Muslim man is not lawful except by one of three meanings: disbelief after faith, adultery after marriage, and killing a soul without right.' As for one who rebels against the ruler, it is permissible to fight him as long as he fights, and if he stops, he is left.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different doctrinal positions on when a Muslim's blood may be lawfully shed. The author first presents the view of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, who list four conditions: committing a major sin (kabira), introducing a religious innovation (bid'a), rebelling against the ruler (sall sayfan 'ala al-sultan), and abandoning a compulsory duty (ta'til farida). They add that if one considers the abandonment lawful (istahalla tarkaha), then his blood is lawful by consensus. Then the author presents the position of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, who restrict permissibility to three conditions based on a hadith: apostasy after faith (kufr ba'd iman), adultery after marriage (zina ba'd ihsan), and murder without right (qatl nafs bi-ghayr haqq). Finally, the author addresses the case of rebellion (baghi): it is permissible to fight a rebel only as long as he is fighting; if he desists, fighting must stop. This passage highlights the Sunni emphasis on preserving Muslim life and restricting killing to explicit textual evidence.

Key Terms
دماء أهل القبلة
المعتزلة
الخوارج
أهل السنة
بدعة
الكبيرة
كفر بعد إيمان
زنا بعد إحصان
قتل نفس
باغي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: According to Ahl al-Sunnah, committing a major sin makes a Muslim's blood lawful.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah restrict lawful killing to three specific causes from the hadith: apostasy, adultery after marriage, and murder without right.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunnah as presented in this passage, which of the following is NOT a valid reason for shedding a Muslim's blood?
A) Apostasy after faith
B) Adultery after marriage
C) Killing someone unjustly
D) Abandoning a prayer without considering it permissible

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah base the permissibility of taking a Muslim's life on explicit hadith evidence, limiting it to apostasy, adultery after marriage, and murder, while rejecting broader grounds proposed by other sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the strict conditions for taking a Muslim's life, do you ever find yourself harboring thoughts that another Muslim 'deserves' harm because of their sins or beliefs? How can you purify your heart to see every Muslim as inviolable, regardless of your disagreements?
?
The Khawarij and Mu'tazila added 'major sin' and 'innovation' as grounds for killing. Is there a person whose actions you consider so grave that you might wish punishment upon them? How does the Prophet's ﷺ hadith challenge that desire and call you to protect, not condemn, the blood of your brothers and sisters?
?
Rebellion is only met with fighting during combat, not execution afterward. Reflect on a conflict in your life—with family, community, or even within yourself. Are you willing to stop fighting when the other stops? Or do you seek to 'finish' them? What would it mean to truly live by the principle that Muslim blood is sacred, even in your most heated disagreements?

Mu'tazila and Khawarij View on Lawful Muslim Blood
Nasafi reports the Mu'tazila and Khawarij position that Muslim blood becomes lawful under four conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four conditions under which the Mu'tazila and Khawarij considered it permissible to shed the blood of a Muslim.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila and Khawarij believed Muslim blood could be lawful for four specific reasons.

This passage presents the view of two early sects on a grave matter: when is it allowed to kill a Muslim? The Mu'tazilites and Kharijites, despite their differences, agreed on four conditions that make a Muslim's blood lawful. Understanding their position highlights the Sunni stance on the inviolability of Muslim life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conditions
Read the Arabic passage and find the four conditions listed by the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. Note the wording for each.
The conditions are numbered in the text: one, second, third, fourth.
2
Understand the nuance of istihlal
Pay attention to the final sentence about istihlal (considering abandonment lawful). The author adds that there is consensus (ijma') on permissibility in that case.
This shows that not all abandonment is treated equally.
3
Compare to Sunni view
Reflect on how this differs from the Sunni position that Muslim blood is generally sacred except in specific cases like hudud or qisas.
The Mu'tazila and Khawarij allow capital punishment for sins beyond what Sunnis allow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة والخوارج : دماء أهل القبلة تحل بإحدى معانٍ أربعة: أحدها: إذا ارتكب الكبيرة. والثاني: إذا أحدث بدعة. والثالث: إذا سل سيفًا علي السلطان. والرابع: إذا عطّل فريضة أي إذا تركها، أما إذا استحل تركها فيحل دمه بالإجماع.
The Mu'tazila and Khawarij said: The blood of the people of the qibla becomes permissible by one of four meanings: One: if he commits a major sin. Second: if he introduces an innovation (bid'a). Third: if he draws a sword against the ruler. Fourth: if he neglects an obligatory duty (fard), i.e., if he abandons it. As for if he considers its abandonment permissible (istihlal), his blood becomes permissible by consensus.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage states the position of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij on when it is lawful to shed the blood of a Muslim (Ahl al-Qibla). They list four conditions: (1) committing a major sin (kabira), (2) introducing a religious innovation (bid'a), (3) rebelling against the ruler by drawing a weapon, and (4) abandoning an obligatory act (fard). The author adds a nuance: if the abandonment is accompanied by istihlal (considering it lawful to abandon), then there is consensus (ijma') that blood becomes permissible. This shows the Mu'tazila/Khawarij allow capital punishment for sins beyond what the Sunnis allow.

Key Terms
دماء أهل القبلة
المعتزلة
الخوارج
الكبيرة
البدعة
السيف على السلطان
تعطيل الفريضة
الإستحلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Khawarij are presented as holding identical views on these four conditions.
Correction: Historically, the positions of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij differed on some details, but Nasafi here presents them as agreeing on these four conditions.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth condition under which the Mu'tazila and Khawarij say the blood of a Muslim becomes permissible?
A) Committing a major sin
B) Introducing an innovation
C) Neglecting an obligatory duty
D) Rebelling against the ruler

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and Khawarij held that Muslim blood could be shed for major sin, innovation, rebellion, or neglect of duty, with consensus if the neglect is deemed lawful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you see a fellow Muslim neglecting prayers, do you ever think they deserve something severe? This lesson reveals a dangerous mindset. Where in your life do you slide from concern to condemnation? Commit today to replace judgment with dua.
?
The Khawarij considered bid'a a capital offense. Have you ever dismissed someone's innovation so completely that you wished them harm? Confront that in yourself. What step can you take tonight to purify your heart toward those you disagree with?

The Three Conditions for Lawful Blood in Sunni Islam
Nasafi cites the prophetic hadith for three capital offenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three specific conditions from the hadith that make a Muslim's blood lawful according to Ahl al-Sunna.

Core Idea
Muslim blood is sacred; only three capital offenses permit it.

This lesson examines the foundational hadith that defines when a Muslim may be subject to capital punishment. The Sunni position is based on the Prophet's ﷺ explicit statement limiting lawful blood to three cases: apostasy, adultery after marriage, and premeditated murder.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the inclusive wording 'except by one of three meanings'.
2
Identify the three conditions
List the three conditions: apostasy, zina after ihsan, unrightful murder. Understand each term.
Ihsan means being in a valid marriage.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the Sunnis emphasize this hadith as the primary criterion for capital punishment.
This restricts extrajudicial killing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أهل السنة والجماعة : دماء أهل القبلة لا تحل إلا بإحدى معانٍ ثلاثة بالحديث وهو ما رُوي عن النبي أنه قال: «لا يحل دم امرئ مسلم إلا بإحدى ثلاث معان: كفر بعد إيمان، وزنا بعد إحصان، وقتل نفس بغير حق». وأما إذا خرج باغيًا على السلطان يجوز قتاله ما دام يقاتله، فإذا ترك يُترك لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِن طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا فَأَصْلِحُوا بَيْنَهُمَا الآية [الحجرات: ٩]. وكذلك إذا وُجِدَ منه الفساد في الأرض مثل اللُّصوص وقطاع الطريق بقوله تعالى: إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ، وَيَسْعَوْنَ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَسَادًا ﴾ الآية [المائدة: ٣٣]. فنقول : دماء أهل القبلة لا تحل إلا بما ذكرنا ، أو أن يوجد منه الفساد في الأرض، بأن كان خناقاً [ غير مرة ] أو قصد مال غيره أو نفسه، أو كان مبتدعا إمامًا في ذلك يدعو الناس إلي البدعة، ويتولد منه الفساد.
And Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: The blood of the people of the qibla is not permissible except by one of three meanings, by the hadith - which is what is narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'The blood of a Muslim man is not lawful except by one of three meanings: disbelief after faith, adultery after iḥṣān (marriage), and killing a soul without right.' As for if he rebels (baghā) against the ruler, it is permissible to fight him as long as he fights, and if he desists, he is left alone, due to His statement: 'And if two parties among the believers fight, make peace between them...' [Al-Hujurat: 9]. And similarly if corruption (fasād) is found from him on earth, like thieves and highway robbers, due to His statement: 'Indeed the penalty for those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger and strive upon earth [spreading] corruption...' [Al-Ma'ida: 33]. So we say: The blood of the people of the qibla is not permissible except by what we mentioned, or if corruption is found from him on earth, such as if he is a strangler [repeat offender] or intends the property or life of another, or if he is an innovation-leading imam who calls people to innovation and corruption arises from it.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni position on when it is allowed to shed the blood of a Muslim. The primary basis is a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ that lists three capital offenses: (1) apostasy (kufr after iman), (2) adultery after ihsan (being married), and (3) premeditated murder without right. Then the author discusses two additional scenarios: rebellion against a rightful ruler and spreading corruption on earth. For rebellion, it is permitted to fight the rebels only while they are fighting; once they cease, they are not to be pursued, based on Qur'an 49:9. For corruption, such as banditry, the penalty is based on Qur'an 5:33. The author concludes by reaffirming the three hadith conditions and adding that if corruption arises from someone who is an innovative leader calling to bid‘a and generating corruption, then it also becomes permissible to take action. This shows that the Sunni approach is restrictive regarding capital punishment for sin, but allows combat for rebellion and corruption, following the Qur'anic injunctions.

Key Terms
أهل السنة والجماعة
لا يحل دم امرئ مسلم
كفر بعد إيمان
زنا بعد إحصان
قتل نفس بغير حق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited by the Sunnis, which of the following is NOT one of the three conditions that make a Muslim's blood lawful?
A) Disbelief after faith
B) Adultery after marriage
C) Rebellion against the ruler
D) Killing a soul without right

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position restricts capital punishment to three specific offenses from the Prophet's hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that a Muslim's blood is sacred unless one of these three conditions is met, how does that change the way you view your own life and the lives of your fellow Muslims? What specific actions will you take today to honour that sacredness?
?
The condition 'kufr after iman' shows that disbelief after belief can cost you your life—spiritually and legally. Have you ever considered that your faith is not simply a label but a covenant that can be broken? What are you doing to guard against that?
?
The hadith mentions 'zina after ihsan'—a sin that incurs the ultimate penalty for the married person. How does this reflect the seriousness of marital betrayal in Islam? In your relationships (or within your marriage), are you upholding the trust that prevents such boundaries from being crossed?

Rebellion and Corruption: Additional Cases for Lawful Blood
Nasafi discusses rebellion and corruption as additional grounds for lawful bloodshed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars extend lawful bloodshed to cases of rebellion against the ruler and spreading corruption on earth, based on Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Rebellion and corruption, while not capital crimes, justify combat according to the Quran.

After establishing the three capital offenses from the hadith, Nasafi addresses two additional situations: rebellion against a just ruler and widespread corruption. He cites Quranic verses for each and elaborates on specific forms of corruption such as banditry and innovation leading to corruption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on rebellion
Read the section on rebellion. Note that fighting is only allowed while the rebel fights; when he desists, he is left alone.
Quran 49:9 is cited to support this.
2
Read the passage on corruption
Read the section on corruption (thieves, highway robbers) and the additional details about repeat offenders and innovation-leading imams.
Quran 5:33 is the basis.
3
Understand the overall ruling
The conclusion reaffirms that blood is not lawful except by the three hadith conditions or by corruption. Consider how this fits with the broader Sunni approach.

For rebellion, it is permitted to fight the rebels only while they are fighting; once they cease, they are not to be pursued, based on Qur'an 49:9. For corruption, such as banditry, the penalty is based on Qur'an 5:33. The author concludes by reaffirming the three hadith conditions and adding that if corruption arises from someone who is an innovative leader calling to bid‘a and generating corruption, then it also becomes permissible to take action. This shows that the Sunni approach is restrictive regarding capital punishment for sin, but allows combat for rebellion and corruption, following the Qur'anic injunctions.

Key Terms
باغي
الفساد في الأرض
اللصوص
قطاع الطريق
مبتدع إمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that rebellion itself makes a Muslim's blood permissible for capital punishment without restriction.
Correction: Rebellion only permits fighting the rebel while he is actively fighting; once he desists, he is left alone. It is not a capital offense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for fighting a rebel (bāghī) against the ruler?
A) The rebel must be killed immediately
B) The rebel is fought as long as he fights, and left alone when he desists
C) The rebel is fought only if he is a non-Muslim
D) The rebel is never to be fought

Key Takeaway
Rebellion and corruption are additional grounds for lawful bloodshed, but with specific conditions: rebellion only while fighting, and corruption requires clear Quranic basis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a Muslim leader you disagree with, does your heart ever wish to rebel? This passage says fighting is only allowed while they fight you—how do you reconcile your feelings with this command? Will you commit to restraint even when you feel angry?
?
Corruption on earth includes taking property or life unlawfully. Have you ever justified harming another Muslim because of their sin, even in your heart? Where do you draw the line between rightful anger and the unlawful desire to shed blood?
?
The passage mentions an innovation-leading imam. Have you ever considered someone's blood lawful because they hold different beliefs? This text forbids that. Will you examine your heart for any trace of takfir, and repent if found?

Textual Designation of Imamate Refuted
Nasafi distinguishes between specific and absolute executorship to refute the Rafidah claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that the imamate was not textually designated for Ali and the refutation of the Rafidah claim based on the distinction between specific and absolute executorship.

Core Idea
Ali was executor only for the Prophet's debts, not for all affairs.

The Rafidah (Shi'a) claim that the imamate was explicitly designated for Ali relies on the notion that Ali was the Prophet's executor (wasi). In this passage, Nasafi presents the Sunni counter-argument: the executorship was limited to a specific task—paying debts—and does not imply a general or political succession. This distinction is key to refuting the claim of textual designation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the first two sentences: the Sunni denial and the Rafidah claim. Notice the key term 'mansusa' (textually designated).
2
Understand the Sunni response
Read the third sentence where the Sunni response distinguishes between a specific executor (wasi mukhtass) and an absolute executor (wasi mutlaq). Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for the imamate?
The response shows that being executor for debts does not make Ali the political successor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الإمامة
قال أهل السنة والجماعة الإمامة ليست بمنصوصة لعلي رضي الله عنه ولا لأولاده.
وقالت الرافضة : الإمامة منصوصة لعلي رضي الله عنه وأولاده، والنبي عليه السلام أوصى إليه فكان هو وصيَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم.
وقال أهل السنة والجماعة : كان وصيَّا في شيء مخصوص وهو قضاء ديونه، والوصي في شيءٍ مخصوص لا يكون وصيّاً في الأشياء كلها، وإنما يكون وصيّا في الأشياء كلها أن لو كان وصيّا مطلقا، وعلي رضي الله عنه ما كان وصيّا مطلقا.
قالت المعتزلة: الوصية فرضٌ على كلّ من مات.
وعندنا إذا أصلح أموره، وقضى ديونه فالوصية ليست بفرض، وهو بالخيار إن شاء أوصى وإن شاء لم يُوَصِّ، وإن لم يصلح أموره ولم يقض ديونه فالوصية فرض. والدليل على أن الإمامة ليست بمنصوصة لعلي رضي الله عنه ولا الحسن ولا الحسين؛ لأنها لو كانت منصوصة لنقلها الصحابة إلى التابعين، والتابعون إلى الصالحين، والصالحون إلينا، ولا يُظَنُّ بالصحابة أنهم قصروا في ذلك أن لو كان.
Section on the Imamate. Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: The imamate is not textually designated for Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, nor for his children. And the Rafidah said: The imamate is textually designated for Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and his children, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, bequeathed to him, so he was the executor (wasi) of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. And Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: He was an executor in a specific thing—namely, the payment of his debts. And the executor in a specific thing is not an executor in all things. He would only be an executor in all things if he were an absolute executor (wasi mutlaq). Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, was not an absolute executor. The Mu'tazila said: The bequest (wasiyya) is obligatory upon everyone who dies. In our view, if he has put his affairs in order and paid his debts, then the bequest is not obligatory, and he has the choice: if he wishes, he may bequeath, and if he wishes, he may not bequeath. But if he has not put his affairs in order and has not paid his debts, then the bequest is obligatory. The proof that the imamate is not textually designated for Ali, nor for al-Hasan nor al-Husayn, is that if it were textually designated, the Companions would have transmitted it to the Successors, and the Successors to the righteous, and the righteous to us. And it is not to be supposed that the Companions fell short in that, if it had been so.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Rafidah (Shi'a) claim that the imamate was explicitly designated (mansusa) by the Prophet for Ali and his descendants. The author presents the Sunni position first: the imamate is not textually designated for Ali or his children. The Rafidah counter that it is designated and that Ali was the Prophet's executor (wasi). The Sunni response distinguishes between a specific executorship (wasiyya mukhtassa) and an absolute one (wasiyya mutlaqa). Ali was only executor for paying the Prophet's debts, not for all matters. Therefore, this limited role does not imply political succession. The author then addresses the Mu'tazila, who claim that making a bequest (wasiyya) is obligatory for everyone. The author clarifies that in Sunni teaching, bequest is only obligatory if one has not already settled his affairs and debts; otherwise it is optional. Finally, the author gives the decisive proof against textual designation: if the imamate had been explicitly designated, it would have been transmitted through successive generations (tawatur). Since there is no such transmission, the designation did not occur. The Companions are not to be accused of failing to transmit something so important.

Key Terms
الإمامة
منصوصة
وصي
وصي مطلق
وصي مخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that being the Prophet's executor (wasi) automatically implies political succession.
Correction: Ali's executorship was limited to paying debts, not a general mandate.

Checkpoint
What was the specific task for which Ali was the Prophet's executor?
A) Leading the army
B) Paying his debts
C) Writing the Qur'an
D) Collecting zakat

Key Takeaway
The imamate was not textually designated for Ali; his role as executor was limited to settling debts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a specific responsibility given to someone automatically makes them your leader in all areas of life? Reflect on a time you overgeneralized someone's authority. What would it take for you to critically examine such assumptions when they arise in your understanding of Islam?
?
The Rafidah built a whole doctrine on a single, incomplete reading of the Prophet's bequest. Are there areas of your own belief where you have accepted a conclusion based on partial evidence or one-sided narration? How can you resist the temptation to rush to judgment in matters of faith?
?
The verse says 'Follow the truth, even if it goes against your desires.' This lesson shows that the truth sometimes requires distinguishing precise terms and rejecting false implications. Are you willing to let precise meanings override your emotional attachments? What would change in your life if you did?

Conditions for Obligatory Bequest (Wasiyya)
Nasafi explains the conditions when a bequest is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni ruling on the obligation of making a bequest (wasiyya) and how it differs from the Mu'tazila view.

Core Idea
A bequest is only obligatory if one's affairs and debts are not settled.

After refuting the claim about Ali's executorship, Nasafi addresses a related legal point about the obligation of making a bequest (wasiyya). The Mu'tazila held that bequest is obligatory upon everyone who dies. The Sunni position distinguishes based on whether one has already settled their affairs and debts. This distinction clarifies that bequest is not a blanket obligation but depends on one's situation.

Guided Path
1
Compare the two views
Read the Mu'tazila statement and then the Sunni counter-statement. Notice the condition that determines obligation.
2
Identify the conditions
Look for the two cases: when affairs are settled and when they are not. Understand the ruling in each case.

Proof from Continuous Transmission (Tawatur)
Nasafi uses the argument from continuous transmission to prove the imamate was not textually designated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof from transmission that the imamate was not textually designated for Ali, based on the lack of continuous transmission.

Core Idea
If the imamate had been designated, it would have been transmitted continuously, but it was not.

Nasafi concludes his refutation by providing a decisive logical argument: if the claim of textual designation were true, it would have been transmitted from the Companions to the Successors and down to us through widely accepted reports (tawatur). Since no such transmission exists, the claim is false. This argument also absolves the Companions from the accusation of concealing such an important matter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the sentence starting with 'The proof...' Nasafi states that if the imamate were designated, the Companions would have transmitted it. Why is this a strong argument?
2
Consider the implication
The lack of transmission implies either the Companions failed to convey it (which is not to be supposed) or the designation simply did not occur. The conclusion is that the claim is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Refutation of the Claim that the Companions Disbelieved
Refutes the Rafidah and Shi'a who claim the companions disbelieved by accepting Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni argument that the Companions remained believers after the Prophet's death, and why the burden of proof falls on those who claim otherwise.

Core Idea
Truth is presumed: the Companions' faith was established before the Prophet's death.

This passage addresses a serious accusation made by some Shi'a groups that the Muhajirun and Ansar disbelieved by pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr. Imam al-Nasafi refutes this by appealing to the established consensus on their Islam during the Prophet's lifetime. The argument is straightforward: their faith was already confirmed, so any claim of apostasy requires clear proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Sunni position on imamate
The passage first clarifies that after Ali, the imamate is not divinely appointed for Hasan and Husayn, but is established by consensus based on Abu Bakr's statement that the rulers are from Quraysh.
2
Recognize the opposing views
Identify the Rafidah claim that imamate is textually designated for Hasan and Husayn, and the Shi'a claim that Ali was the rightful caliph and the Companions disbelieved by pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr.
These claims are presented to be refuted.
3
Analyze the Sunni rebuttal
The key argument: there was consensus on the Islam of the Muhajirun and Ansar before the Prophet's death. Therefore, anyone claiming they apostatized after bears the burden of proof.
This shifts the argumentative burden to the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد علي رضي الله عنه، لا نقول بأنَّ الإمامة منصوصة للحسن والحسين، وإنما الإمامة تثبت بإجماع المسلمين بعد أن قال أبو بكر رضي الله عنه : الأئمة من قريش. وقالت الروافض الإمامة منصوصة للحسن والحسين بعد علي رضي الله عنه. وقالت الشيعة بأن عليّا رضي الله عنه كان خليفة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، والمهاجرون والأنصار كفروا بالله حين بايعوا أبا بكرٍ رضي الله عنه. فنقول: انعقد الإجماع على إسلامهم قبل وفاة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فكل من يقول بأنهم كفروا بعد وفاة النبي عليه السلام فعليه الدليل.
And after Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, we do not say that the imamate is textually designated (mansus) for al-Hasan and al-Husayn. Rather, the imamate is established by the consensus of the Muslims after Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'The rulers are from Quraysh.' And the Rafidah said that the imamate is textually designated for al-Hasan and al-Husayn after Ali, may Allah be pleased with him. And the Shi'a said that Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, was the caliph of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and that the Emigrants (muhajirun) and Helpers (ansar) disbelieved in Allah when they pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him. So we say: Consensus was established upon their Islam before the death of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. So whoever says that they disbelieved after the death of the Prophet, peace be upon him, must provide evidence.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation. It first clarifies the Sunni position on the imamate after Ali: it is not divinely appointed (mansus) for al-Hasan and al-Husayn; rather, it is established by consensus (ijma') of the Muslims, based on Abu Bakr's statement that the rulers are from Quraysh. Then it presents the opposing views: the Rafidah claim textual designation for al-Hasan and al-Husayn, and the Shi'a claim that Ali was the rightful caliph and that the Muhajirun and Ansar disbelieved by pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr. The Sunni response is that there was consensus (ijma') on the Islam of the Emigrants and Helpers during the Prophet's lifetime. Therefore, anyone claiming they apostatized after his death bears the burden of proof (al-dalil). This shifts the argumentative burden to the opponent, as apostasy is a serious claim that requires clear evidence.

Key Terms
الإمامة
منصوصة
الإجماع
الروافض
الشيعة
كفروا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this passage denies any virtue of Hasan and Husayn.
Correction: The passage only denies that their imamate is divinely designated; Sunni tradition holds them in high esteem but maintains that rule is determined by consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Sunni proof that the Companions (Muhajirun and Ansar) did not disbelieve?
A) They were all martyrs
B) Their Islam was established by consensus before the Prophet's death
C) The Prophet testified for each of them individually
D) They fought in the Battle of Badr

Key Takeaway
The consensus on the Companions' faith before the Prophet's death means any claim of apostasy afterward requires clear evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made a harsh judgment about another Muslim (or group of Muslims) without having solid proof? How does this lesson's emphasis on the burden of proof challenge you to reconsider your words and attitudes?
?
The Sunni defense of the Companions is based on consensus established before the Prophet's death. Reflect: Is your own faith rooted in the established testimony of the Prophet (sunna and ijma'), or do you rely on your own opinions? What would it take for you to be certain of someone else's faith?
?
This passage reminds us that false accusations of disbelief can be refuted by established truth. Think of a time when you were quick to label someone as misguided. How can you apply the principle of 'whoever claims must bring evidence' to your own speaking?

The Thirty-Year Caliphate and Refutation of Shi'a Claims
Author recounts caliphate durations, cites hadith, and refutes Rawafid/Shi'a claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars defend the legitimacy of the first four caliphs and refute the Shi'a and Rawafid claims of textual designation and apostasy of the Companions.

Core Idea
The caliphate after the Prophet was thirty years, established by consensus, not by explicit text.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi provides a concise historical and theological argument for the legitimacy of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. He cites a well-known hadith on the thirty-year caliphate, clarifies that imamate after Ali is based on consensus rather than explicit designation, and firmly refutes the claims of the Rawafid and Shi'a that the Emigrants and Helpers became disbelievers. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from historical facts to doctrine to refutation.
Pay attention to the hadith on caliphate duration.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents the Sunni position on the caliphate, then the Rawafid view, then the Shi'a view. Find each and note the key differences.
The Rawafid claim nass for Hasan and Husayn; the Shi'a claim Ali was the rightful caliph and the Companions apostatised.
3
Understand the Sunni refutation
The author refutes the Shi'a claim by pointing to the consensus on the Companions' Islam before the Prophet's death. The burden of proof is on those who claim apostasy.
4
Reflect on the role of consensus
Note that Sunni theology grounds the imamate in consensus (ijma'), not in a written text. This is a key distinctive from Shi'a views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رضي الله عنه سنتين، وخلافة علي رضي الله عنه ست سنين، فذلك كله ثلاثون سنة، وعن النبي أنه قال: «الخلافة من بعدي ثلاثون سنة، ثم تصير إمارة وملكاً». وبعد علي رضي الله عنه، لا نقول بأن الإمامة منصوصة للحسن والحسين، وإنما الإمامة تثبت بإجماع المسلمين بعد أن قال أبو بكر رضي الله عنه: الأئمة من قريش. وقالت الروافض الإمامة منصوصة للحسن والحسين بعد علي رضي الله عنه. وقالت الشيعة بأن علياً رضي الله عنه كان خليفة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، والمهاجرون والأنصار كفروا بالله حين بايعوا أبا بكرٍ رضي الله عنه. فنقول: انعقد الإجماع على إسلامهم قبل وفاة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فكل من يقول بأنهم كفروا بعد وفاة النبي عليه السلام فعليه الدليل.
May Allah be pleased with him for two years, and the caliphate of Ali may Allah be pleased with him was six years, so all that is thirty years. And from the Prophet that he said: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years, then it becomes an emirate and kingship.' And after Ali may Allah be pleased with him, we do not say that the imamate is textually designated for al-Hasan and al-Husayn, but rather the imamate is established by the consensus of the Muslims after Abu Bakr may Allah be pleased with him said: 'The imams are from Quraysh.' And the Rawafid said that the imamate is textually designated for al-Hasan and al-Husayn after Ali may Allah be pleased with him. And the Shi'a said that Ali may Allah be pleased with him was the caliph of the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم, and the Emigrants and Helpers disbelieved in Allah when they pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr may Allah be pleased with him. So we say: Consensus was established on their Islam before the death of the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم, so whoever says that they disbelieved after the death of the Prophet عليه السلام must bring proof.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a combined statement and refutation. The author first recounts the durations of the caliphates of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali, totaling thirty years, and cites the Prophet's hadith that the caliphate after him would be thirty years. This establishes the legitimacy of the first four caliphs. Then the author clarifies the Sunni position on imamate after Ali: it is not based on explicit designation (nass) for Hasan and Husayn, but rather on consensus (ijma') of the Muslims, as initiated by Abu Bakr's statement that the imams are from Quraysh. The author then mentions the opposing views: the Rawafid (a term for extreme Shi'a) claim nass for Hasan and Husayn, and the Shi'a claim that Ali was the rightful caliph and that the Emigrants and Helpers who pledged to Abu Bakr became disbelievers. The author refutes this by pointing out that the consensus on the Islam of the Emigrants and Helpers existed before the Prophet's death, so anyone who claims they disbelieved afterwards must provide proof. The argument is that the burden of proof lies on those who make the claim of apostasy against the Companions.

Correction: The author shows that consensus on the Companions' Islam was already established before the Prophet's death, so the claim of apostasy requires proof.

Checkpoint

B) By consensus (ijma') of the Muslims

Key Takeaway
The legitimacy of the Rightly Guided Caliphate is based on prophetic hadith and consensus, not on textual designation, and accusations of apostasy against the Companions require proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you find yourself questioning their sincerity, what proof do you have that overturns the unanimous testimony of the entire community? Ponder this: your opinion versus the consensus of the best generation—who is more likely correct? Commit to purifying your heart of any suspicion against them.
?
The Prophet said the caliphate would last thirty years and then become kingship. Look at the Muslim world today—do you still expect to see a perfect Islamic state free of corruption? Or do you believe that God allowed this change as a test? How much of your dissatisfaction with current Muslim rulers stems from an unrealistic expectation that leadership should be like the Rashidun? Repent of any rebellion against authority that arises from a flawed understanding of prophecy.

Evidence for Abu Bakr's Superiority among the Companions
Author presents evidence from Ali's testimony and the Prophet's actions that Abu Bakr is the best of the Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence from Ali's testimony and the Prophet's actions that demonstrate Abu Bakr's superiority over the other Companions.

Core Idea
Ali's naming of Abu Bakr first and the Prophet's actions prove his merit.

In this passage, the author presents two key proofs that Abu Bakr is the best of the Companions. The first is a famous incident where Ali explicitly names Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman in order, implying their superiority. The second is the Prophet's practice of seating Abu Bakr on his right and appointing him as imam in his final illness, indicating merit. Let's examine the source text and commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Notice the two distinct arguments the author makes.
The passage is divided into two evidentiary sections.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
First, the narration from Ali. Second, the Prophet's seating and appointment of Abu Bakr. Understand how each points to Abu Bakr's superiority.
The author also argues that hypocrisy is not possible from the Prophet.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why Ali's silence is explained as avoiding self-praise, and why the Prophet's actions must be based on merit, not hypocrisy.
This connects to the broader theological principle that the Prophet's actions are truthful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان أن أفضل الصحابة رضي الله عنهم أبو بكر رضي الله عنه يدل عليه أن عليا رضي الله عنه كان يخطب على منبر الكوفة، فقال ابنه محمد بن الحنفية: من خير هذه الأمة بعد رسول الله؟ فقال: أبو بكر، قال: ثم من؟ قال عمر، قال: ثم من؟ قال: عثمان، قال: ثم من؟ فسكت علي رضي الله عنه. فقال: لو شئت لأنبأتكم بالرابع، فقال محمد بن الحنفية: أنت، فقال علي: أبوك امرؤ من المسلمين. وإنما سكت علي رضي الله عنه؛ لأنه لم يرد أن يمدح نفسه. ويدل عليه أن النبي كان يجلس أبا بكر عن يمينه، وعمر عن يساره فلا يخلو إما أن جعل ذلك نفاقاً أو استحقاقاً، ولا يظن بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فعل ذلك نفاقاً، لأنه لا يخاف منهما، وكذلك كانا يقومان بحذائه، وكذلك استخلفه في آخر عمره. فدل أنه فعل ذلك استحقاقاً، لأنه استخلفه بحضرة جميع الصحابة، بخلاف استخلاف ابن أم مكتوم، لأن الصحابة كانوا بالغزو مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، والله الهادي إلى سبيل الرشاد.
Chapter on explaining that the best of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, is Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him. Evidence for this is that Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, was delivering a sermon on the pulpit of Kufa, and his son Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah asked: 'Who is the best of this nation after the Messenger of Allah?' He said: 'Abu Bakr.' He said: 'Then who?' He said: 'Umar.' He said: 'Then who?' He said: 'Uthman.' He said: 'Then who?' So Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, remained silent. Then he said: 'If I wished I would inform you of the fourth.' So Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah said: 'You.' So Ali said: 'Your father is a man among the Muslims.' And Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, remained silent only because he did not want to praise himself. And evidence for it is that the Prophet used to seat Abu Bakr on his right and Umar on his left, and this is either done out of hypocrisy or merit, and it is not thought that the Prophet, peace be upon him, did that out of hypocrisy, because he did not fear them. Likewise, they used to stand facing him, and likewise he appointed him as successor (istakhalfa) at the end of his life. So this indicates that he did that out of merit, because he appointed him as successor in the presence of all the Companions, unlike the appointment of Ibn Umm Maktum, because the Companions were on a military expedition with the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم. And Allah is the Guide to the path of right guidance.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a proof-text chapter establishing that Abu Bakr is the best of the Companions. The author provides two main pieces of evidence. First, a narrated incident involving Ali: while Ali was giving a sermon in Kufa, his son Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah asked him to name the best of the ummah after the Prophet. Ali named Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman. When pressed for the fourth, Ali remained silent, then hinted he could name the fourth if he wished. His son suggested it was Ali himself, but Ali deflected by saying 'Your father is a man among the Muslims,' thereby avoiding self-praise. The author explains that Ali's silence was to avoid self-praise, not because he doubted his own rank; but his explicit naming of the first three in order establishes their superiority over him. Second, the author notes that the Prophet used to seat Abu Bakr on his right and Umar on his left. This cannot be out of hypocrisy (because the Prophet did not fear them), so it must be out of merit. Furthermore, the Prophet appointed Abu Bakr as his successor (imam) for prayer in his final illness, in the presence of all the Companions, which indicates his merit over others. The author contrasts this with the Prophet's appointment of Ibn Umm Maktum to lead prayer during a military campaign, which was done while the Companions were away, showing it was a practical arrangement, not a statement of merit. Thus, the cumulative evidence proves Abu Bakr's superiority.

Key Terms
أفضل الصحابة
استخلاف
نفاق
استحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's silence means he was the best but didn't say so.
Correction: Ali remained silent to avoid self-praise, not because he doubted the order. His naming of the first three establishes their superiority over him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Ali remain silent when asked about the fourth best person?
A) He did not know who it was
B) He did not want to praise himself
C) He was angry with the question
D) He forgot the name

Key Takeaway
The authority of Abu Bakr is established through explicit testimony from Ali and prophetic actions, both indicating his merit over the other Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ali avoided praising himself even when it was clear he was the fourth best. When are you tempted to seek praise or recognition for your own virtues, and how can you emulate Ali's humility in those moments?
?
The Prophet's actions, like seating Abu Bakr on his right, are presented as clear evidence of merit. Do you truly accept the Prophet's choices and judgments in your own life, or do you sometimes resist them because they conflict with your personal preferences?
?
The lesson asserts that the Prophet did not act out of hypocrisy because he feared no one. Examine your own intentions: when you act favorably toward someone, is it out of genuine merit or because you fear their opinion or seek their approval? What would change if you acted only out of sincerity before Allah?

Ali's Testimony on Abu Bakr's Superiority
Narration of Ali's ranking and silence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ali's public testimony, including his deliberate silence, establishes Abu Bakr as the best companion.

Core Idea
Ali's silence was not doubt but modesty—proving Abu Bakr's status.

The first proof Nasafi presents is the testimony of Ali, the fourth Caliph. During a sermon in Kufa, his son asked him to rank the best after the Prophet. Ali's response and subsequent silence provide a clear indication of Abu Bakr's excellence. Let's examine this narration closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about Ali's testimony
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the dialogue between Ali and his son.
2
Understand Ali's silence
Nasafi explains why Ali remained silent—he did not want to praise himself. This implies he was the fourth, confirming the order.
This counters the misunderstanding that Ali disagreed.
3
Appreciate the argumentative force
The testimony of a major companion like Ali is strong evidence for the Ahl al-Sunnah position.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Ali's modesty actually reinforces Abu Bakr's superiority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان أن أفضل الصحابة رضي الله عنهم أبو بكر رضي الله عنه ] يدل عليه أن عليا رضي الله عنه كان يخطب على منبر الكوفة، فقال ابنه محمد بن الحنفية: من خير هذه الأمة بعد رسول الله ؟ فقال : أبو بكر، قال: ثم من؟ قال عمر ، قال : ثم من؟ قال: عثمان، قال: ثم من؟ فسكت علي رضي الله عنه. فقال: لو شئت لأنبأتكم بالرابع، فقال محمد بن الحنفية: أنت، فقال علي: أبوك امرؤ من المسلمين. وإنما سكت على رضي الله عنه؛ لأنه لم يرد أن يمدح نفسه. ويدل عليه أن النبي كان يجلس أبا بكر عن يمينه، وعمر عن يساره فلا يخلو إما أن جعل ذلك نفاقا أو استحقاقا، ولا يظن بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فعل ذلك نفاقا، لأنه لا يخاف منهما، وكذلك كانا يقومان بحذائه، وكذلك استخلفه في آخر عمره. فدل أنه فعل ذلك استحقاقاً، لأنه استخلفه بحضرة جميع الصحابة، بخلاف استخلاف ابن أم مكتوم، لأن الصحابة كانوا بالغزو مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، والله الهادي إلى سبيل الرشاد ]
Chapter on stating that the best of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, is Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him. Evidence for it: Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, was delivering a sermon on the pulpit of Kufa, and his son Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah said: Who is the best of this nation after the Messenger of Allah? He said: Abu Bakr. He said: Then who? He said: Umar. He said: Then who? He said: Uthman. He said: Then who? Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, remained silent. Then he said: If I wished, I would inform you of the fourth. Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah said: You. Ali said: Your father is a man from among the Muslims. Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, only remained silent because he did not wish to praise himself. Evidence also: The Prophet used to seat Abu Bakr on his right and Umar on his left. This cannot be other than either done out of hypocrisy or merit. It cannot be thought that the Prophet, may Allah's peace and blessings be upon him, did that out of hypocrisy, because he did not fear them. Likewise, they would stand facing him, and likewise he appointed him as successor at the end of his life. So it indicates that he did that as a matter of merit, because he appointed him as successor in the presence of all the Companions, unlike the appointment of Ibn Umm Maktum, because the Companions were away on campaign with the Messenger of Allah, may Allah's peace and blessings be upon him. And Allah is the Guide to the path of rectitude.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative argument proving that Abu Bakr is the most excellent of the Companions. The author (Nasafi) presents two main evidences. First, the testimony of Ali ibn Abi Talib. During a sermon in Kufa, Ali's son asked him to name the best after the Prophet; Ali listed Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and when pressed for the fourth, he remained silent but hinted he could name him. His son suggested 'You', but Ali deflected, saying 'Your father is a man from among the Muslims.' Nasafi explains that Ali's silence was not due to doubt but because he did not want to praise himself, which implies that he considered himself the fourth best, thus confirming Abu Bakr as the best. Second, the actions of the Prophet: he seated Abu Bakr on his right and Umar on his left, which can only be either deceit (nifaaq) or recognition of merit (istiḥqāq). Deceit is impossible for a Prophet, especially since he had nothing to fear from them. Moreover, the Prophet appointed Abu Bakr as his successor (khalīfa) in the presence of all Companions, which was a public endorsement, unlike the temporary appointment of Ibn Umm Maktum during a military campaign. This established that Abu Bakr's superiority is based on divine guidance.

الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Ali's silence meant he disagreed with the order.
Correction: Ali remained silent only to avoid praising himself, thus confirming the order.

Checkpoint
Why did Ali remain silent when asked about the fourth best companion?
A) He forgot the name.
B) He did not want to praise himself.
C) He disagreed with the order.
D) He was not sure.

Key Takeaway
Ali's public ranking and silence confirm Abu Bakr as the best companion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ali's silence was an act of strength. What would it cost you to remain silent when a moment of recognition passes you by? Can you trust that humility is enough?
?
Consider the order of the companions in your heart. Is your devotion to them based on their status before Allah or on your own preferences? Today, what would it take for you to publicly affirm the excellence of those you might privately disagree with?

The Prophet's actions were based on merit, not hypocrisy.

His son suggested 'You', but Ali deflected, saying 'Your father is a man from among the Muslims.' Nasafi explains that Ali's silence was not due to doubt but because he did not want to praise himself, which implies that he considered himself the fourth best, thus confirming Abu Bakr as the best. Second, the actions of the Prophet: he seated Abu Bakr on his right and Umar on his left, which can only be either deceit (nifaaq) or recognition of merit (istiḥqāq). Deceit is impossible for a Prophet, especially since he had nothing to fear from them. Moreover, the Prophet appointed Abu Bakr as his successor (khalīfa) in the presence of all Companions, which was a public endorsement, unlike the temporary appointment of Ibn Umm Maktum during a military campaign. This established that Abu Bakr's superiority is based on divine guidance.

Key Terms
الاستحقاق
النفاق
استخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's seating was just a casual habit, not indicative of merit.
Correction: Nasafi proves it was deliberate and based on merit, not hypocrisy.

Checkpoint
Why can't the Prophet's seating of Abu Bakr on his right be out of hypocrisy?
A) Because the Prophet feared no one.
B) Because hypocrites don't sit on the right.
C) Because Abu Bakr was Umar's superior.
D) Because the Prophet was unaware.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's public seating and appointment of Abu Bakr demonstrate his merit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet publicly seated Abu Bakr on his right and appointed him successor, and you know it was out of merit. In your own interactions, do you give people their due positions based on merit, or do personal feelings and biases interfere? What is one specific step you can take today to be more just in this regard?
?
If you had been a companion witnessing the Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr, would your heart have accepted it fully, or would you have harbored any doubt? What current attitudes in your life toward the Prophet's choices for the early community might need purification?
?
The Prophet did not act hypocritically because he did not fear anyone. Are there times when you act differently because you fear someone's opinion? How can you model your sincerity after the Prophet's example, especially in matters of truth and justice?

Refuting the Shia Claim of Ali's Return from the Hadith of Position
Nasafi refutes the Shia inference from the manzilat Harun hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Shia extremist claim that Imam Ali will return by analyzing the manzilat Harun hadith in its proper context and exposing the flaw in the analogy.

Core Idea
A hadith about comfort, not a prophecy of return.

The extremist Shia claim that Imam Ali will return before the Day of Judgment is often supported by the Prophet's statement 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses.' Nasafi responds by explaining the actual occasion of the hadith and exposing the logical gap in their reasoning. Let us examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Understand the flow: an objection followed by a two-part answer.
The passage is an objection-and-answer structure common in kalam.
2
Understand the Shia inference
The objector argues that since Aaron's succession to Moses was permanent and without change, Ali's position must also be permanent, implying he did not die and will return.
Note the key premise: 'khalafat Harun lam yakun laha tabdil.'
3
See Nasafi's first response: Context
Nasafi explains that the hadith was said to console Ali when hypocrites criticized him for being left behind in Medina. The Prophet's words were situational comfort, not a political appointment.
The virtue of Ali remains, but the inference is wrong.
4
See Nasafi's second response: Flawed analogy
Nasafi points out that Aaron died before Moses, so the comparison actually suggests Ali would die before the Prophet. If permanent succession were meant, the Prophet would have compared Ali to Joshua, who succeeded Moses after his death.
This refutes the claim of return directly.
5
Reflect on the effectiveness
Consider how Nasafi uses both context and internal logic to refute the objection without undermining Ali's virtue.
This is a model of respectful but firm polemic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الرد على غلاة الشيعة القائلين بعودة الإمام علي عليه السلام] فإن قيل: روي عن النبي أنه قال لعلي رضي الله عنه: «أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى عليه السلام إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي » وخلافة هارون لم يكن لها تبديل فكذلك ههنا. والجواب عنه أن نقول: فضيلته لم تكن من الوجه الذي توهمتم لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم استخلفه على المدينة وخرج إلى بعض الغزوات، فقال المنافقون: النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أعرض عنه وحبسه في البيت، فاغتم بذلك علي رضي الله عنه، فقال له النبي عليه السلام: «أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى عليه السلام يدل عليه أن هارون مات قبل موسى عليه السلام، وإنما يصح هذا أن لو قال: أنت مني بمنزلة يوشع بن نون عليه السلام، وقد كان خليفة موسى عليه السلام [يومين]
Chapter on refuting the extremist Shiites who claim the return of Imam Ali, peace be upon him. If it is said: It is narrated from the Prophet that he said to Ali, may Allah be pleased with him: 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses, peace be upon them, except that there is no prophet after me.' And the succession of Aaron was not subject to change, so likewise here. The answer to that is that we say: His virtue was not in the manner you imagined, because the Prophet, may Allah's peace and blessings be upon him, appointed him as successor over Medina and went out on some campaigns. The hypocrites said: The Prophet turned away from him and confined him at home, so Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, was grieved by that. So the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to him: 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses, peace be upon them.' Evidence for that is that Aaron died before Moses, peace be upon him. And this would be correct only if he had said: 'You are to me as Joshua bin Nun, peace be upon him,' for he was the successor of Moses, peace be upon him, for two days.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument against the Shia extremist claim that Ali will return (raj'a) based on the hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses.' The objection: Since Aaron's succession to Moses was permanent (no change), Ali's position should likewise be permanent, implying he did not die but will return. The author (Nasafi) provides two parts to the answer. First, he explains the original context: the Prophet appointed Ali over Medina during a campaign, and hypocrites criticized Ali, causing him distress. The Prophet's statement was to console Ali, not to establish a permanent caliphate. Second, the author refutes the analogy itself: Aaron died before Moses, so the comparison would actually undermine the claim of Ali's return, because Aaron did not outlive Moses to succeed him permanently after his death. If the Prophet had meant permanent succession, he would have compared Ali to Joshua bin Nun, who succeeded Moses (for two days according to tradition). Therefore, the hadith does not support the Shia contention. The answer preserves the virtue of Ali but denies the inference of his physical return.

Key Terms
غلاة الشيعة
عودة الإمام علي
منزلة هارون
يوشع بن نون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' proves Ali's permanent succession and thus his return.
Correction: The hadith was a situational comfort, and the analogy with Aaron (who died before Moses) actually undermines the claim of return.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the hadith does not prove Ali's return?
A) Because Ali died before the Prophet.
B) Because Aaron died before Moses, and the hadith compares Ali to Aaron.
C) Because the hadith is weak.
D) Because the Prophet appointed Umar as successor.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of position (manzilat Harun) does not support the Shia claim of Ali's return because it was a situational comfort and the analogy with Aaron actually undermines the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a hadith that seems to support a belief you already hold, do you actively seek its context (asbab al-wurud) or are you content to take it at face value? What could that cost you in terms of truth?
?
The Prophet's words of comfort to Ali were later twisted to support a doctrine Ali himself never claimed. Are there comforting words or teachings from the Quran or Sunnah that you have unconsciously reshaped to fit your own desires rather than submitting to their original intent?
?
If a single analogy (Aaron) can be misinterpreted to produce a major theological error, how seriously do you take the responsibility of understanding the precise wording and occasion of every proof you build your faith upon? Will you commit today to studying the occasions of revelation (asbab al-nuzul and asbab al-wurud) for any text you cite as evidence?
